Refutation of Opposing Sects on Divine Speech
Al-Taftazani enumerates and refutes opposing sects' views on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, and Mu'tazila on divine speech and why the Ash'ari school rejects them.

Core Idea
Only the Ash'ari school correctly identifies divine speech as an eternal attribute; other sects err by either taking it as created, or as composed of temporal parts.

Al-Taftazani now addresses the divergent views of other sects. He states that all other groups oppose the Ash'ari position. He specifies that only the Hanabila and Hashwiyya affirm the pre-eternity of speech but they are ignorant because speech has sequential parts which cannot be eternal. The Karamiyya claim speech is originated but subsists in God's essence. The Mu'tazila claim it is an accident created in a body. This lesson surveys these positions and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the three sects
Read the Arabic and identify the three groups mentioned: Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, Mu'tazila.
The phrase 'جميع الفرق' indicates opposition from all other groups.
2
Understand the Hanabila/Hashwiyya error
They affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech, but this is refuted because speech has sequential parts which cannot be eternal.
The author calls their view 'ignorance or obstinacy'.
3
Understand the Karamiyya error
They claim speech is originated (hadith) but subsists in Allah's essence, and they define it as the power to produce speech.
They call it 'qawl' (statement).
4
Understand the Mu'tazila error
They say speech is an accident created in some body, and Allah's speaking is His creating speech in that body.
5
Note the author's dismissive tone
The author uses strong language ('جهلاً أو عناداً') to dismiss the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, implying the refutation is obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس في أنه متكلم تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام مع ثبوت صدقهم بالمعجزات من غير توقف على الكلام وقد يستدل بأن ضده في الحي نقص أو قصور في الكمال على ما قد مر ثم كلامه عندنا صفة أزلية منافية للسكوت والآفة يدل عليها بالعبارة أو الكتابة ليست من جنس الأصوات والحروف، وخالفنا في ذلك جميع الفرق ذهاباً إلى أن المعقول من الكلام هو الحي دون النفي ولم يقل بقدمه إلا الحنابلة والحشوية جهلاً منهم أو عناداً إذ لا خفاء في ترتب أجزائه وامتناع بقائه وزعم الكرامية أنه مع حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى وسموه قوله وجعلوا كلامه عبارة عن القدرة على إيجاد القول وعند المعتزلة هو حادث في جسم ما، ومعنى تكلم الباري تعالى به خلقه فيه.
The sixth investigation: concerning that He is a speaker (mutakallim). The statement of that has been transmitted continuously (tawatur) from the Prophets, peace be upon them, along with the confirmation of their truthfulness by miracles, without depending on speech. And it may be argued that its opposite in a living being is deficiency or imperfection in perfection, as has passed. Then His speech, in our view, is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) incompatible with silence and defect; it is indicated by expression (ibarah) or writing (kitabah), not being of the genus of sounds and letters. All sects have opposed us in this, holding that the rational (ma'qul) of speech is the living being (al-hayy) without negation. And none said its pre-eternity (qidam) except the Hanabila and Hashwiyya, out of ignorance or obstinacy, since there is no hiddenness in the sequence of its parts and the impossibility of its remaining. And the Karamiyya claimed that it is, while originated (hadith), subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, and they called it His statement (qawl). And they made His speech an expression of the ability to produce speech. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is originated in some body, and the meaning of Allah Most High speaking is His creating it in that body.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a proof and a classification of theological positions regarding the divine attribute of speech (kalam). The author begins with the proof: the fact that God is 'speaking' (mutakallim) is known through the tawatur (massively transmitted reports) of the prophets, whose truthfulness is confirmed by miracles—so this belief does not require direct revelation of speech. Another proof is from the principle of perfection: silence (the opposite of speech) in a living being is a defect, so God must possess speech. The author then defines the Ash'ari position: God's speech is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) that is incompatible with silence and defect. It is not composed of sounds or letters; rather, it is indicated (yadullu 'alayha) by expressions (ibarah) or writing (kitabah). This distinguishes the eternal meaning (ma'na) of speech from the created words that express it. Next, the author surveys the opposing sects: all other groups reject the Ash'ari definition. He specifies that only the Hanabila and Hashwiyya affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech, but they are ignorant or obstinate because speech has sequential parts (ḥurūf wa aṣwāt) which cannot be eternal. The Karamiyya hold that speech is originated (hadith) but subsists in God's essence, and they also identify it with the power to create speech. The Mu'tazila hold that speech is an accident created in some body (e.g., the speech of the tree or the angel), and God's 'speaking' means He creates that speech in another locus. The author implicitly refutes all these views by upholding the Ash'ari position that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of temporal elements.

Key Terms
الحنابلة
الحشوية
الكرامية
المعتزلة
قول
قدرة
حادث
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karamiyya and Mu'tazila have similar views on divine speech.
Correction: Karamiyya say speech is originated but subsists in God's essence, while Mu'tazila say it is an accident created in a body.

Checkpoint
Which sect according to the passage claims that divine speech is originated but subsists in Allah's essence?
A) Hanabila
B) Hashwiyya
C) Karamiyya
D) Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of an eternal, non-composite attribute of speech is defended by refuting the alternative positions of the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, and Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Hanabila and Hashwiyya clung to the pre-eternity of speech 'out of ignorance or obstinacy,' have you ever held a belief about Allah simply because it was handed down to you, without testing it against reason and revelation? Which of your own convictions might need that same scrutiny?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's speech is an eternal, living attribute that directly addresses you—not a created thing—would you approach the Quran differently? What would change in the way you listen to His words each day?
?
The Karamiyya reduced divine speech to mere potential (ability to create speech). Have you ever treated God's commands as abstract ideals rather than real, immediate demands on your life? What step will you take today to treat His word as actually spoken to you right now?

Two Proofs that God is Mutakallim: Prophetic Consensus and Reason
Two proofs for divine speech with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two proofs that God is mutakallim (speaking) — from prophetic consensus (tawātur) and from rational argument — and how the author answers the objection that silence is not necessarily a deficiency.

Core Idea
God's speech is proven by prophets and reason; the speaker is more perfect than the silent.

This passage from al-Sanusi presents the sixth topic: that God is mutakallim. He offers two distinct proofs: first, the unanimous reports of the prophets, verified by miracles; second, a rational argument based on the perfection of speech. He then addresses an objection concerning silence and power to speak. By the end, it becomes clear that divine speech is universally affirmed among religious traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the two distinct proofs and the objection.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the proof from prophetic consensus (تواتر) and the rational proof (دليل عقلي). See how they are structured.
The first relies on tawatur and miracles; the second on analogy with hearing and sight.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Examine the objection that silence might not be a deficiency if one has the power to speak. Understand the author's response that the speaker is still more perfect.
The answer mirrors that given for hearing and sight.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the final sentence: there is no disagreement among religious groups that God is mutakallim.
This emphasizes the universal consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : في أنه متكلم ، تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء ، وقد ثبت صدقهم بدلالة المعجزات من غير توقف على إخبار الله تعالى عن صدقهم بطريق التكلم ليلزم الدور، وقد يستدل على ذلك بدليل عقلي على قياس ما مرّ في السمع والبصر، وهو أن عدم التكلم ممن يصح اتصافه بالكلام أعني الحي العالم القادر نقص، واتصاف بأضداد الكلام، وهو على الله تعالى محال . وإن نوقش في كونه نقصاً، سيما إذا كان مع قدرة على الكلام ، كما في السكوت فلا خفاء في أن المتكلم أكمل من غيره. ويمتنع أن يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق والاعتراض. والجواب : هنا كما مرّ في السمع والبصر وبالجملة لا خلاف لأرباب الملل والمذاهب في كون الباري تعالى متكلماً.
The sixth topic: concerning that He is mutakallim (speaking). The statement of that has been recurrently reported (tawātara) from the prophets, and their truthfulness has been established by the indication of miracles without depending on God, exalted be He, informing of their truthfulness by way of speech, lest circularity occur. And one may infer that by a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) analogously to what has preceded in [the topics of] hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar), which is that the absence of speech from one whom it is correct to describe with speech—I mean the living, knowing, powerful being—is a deficiency (nuqṣ), and his being described with the opposites of speech is impossible (muḥāl) for God, exalted be He. And if it is disputed that it is a deficiency, especially if it is accompanied by the power to speak, as in silence, then it is not hidden that the speaker is more perfect (akmal) than the non-speaker. And it is impossible that the creature be more perfect than the Creator; that is an objection. And the answer is here as has preceded in [the topics of] hearing and sight. In summary, there is no disagreement among the adherents of religions and schools concerning that the Creator, exalted be He, is mutakallim.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a proof passage containing two arguments: a proof from prophetic consensus and a rational proof, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by restating the sixth topic: that God is mutakallim. The first proof is the prophetic consensus (tawātur): the prophets unanimously reported this, and their truthfulness is established by miracles (muʿjizāt) without needing God's own speech to confirm them, thus avoiding circular reasoning (al-dawr). The second proof is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) similar to that used for God's attributes of hearing and sight. It runs as follows: the absence of speech (ʿadam al-takallum) from a being who is living (ḥayy), knowing (ʿālim), and powerful (qādir) is a deficiency (nuqṣ), and being described with opposites of speech is impossible for God. If one objects that silence is not a deficiency when one has the power to speak, the author responds that the speaker (mutakallim) is still more perfect than the non-speaker, and it is impossible for a creature to be more perfect than the Creator. The answer is the same as given previously for hearing and sight. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement among religious groups that God is mutakallim.

Key Terms
تواتر
دليل عقلي
نقص
دور
معجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from prophecy is circular because it relies on God's speech to confirm the prophets.
Correction: The author clarifies that the prophets' truthfulness is established by miracles, not by God's speech, thus no circularity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the proof from prophecy does not lead to circularity (dawr)?
A) Because the prophets are believed on their own authority.
B) Because the truthfulness of the prophets is confirmed by miracles without relying on God's speech.
C) Because everyone already agrees God speaks.
D) Because the prophets are infallible by consensus.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is established by both prophetic consensus and rational necessity, and any objection based on the perfection of silence is answered by the greater perfection of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is a speaking God, and His speech is a perfection, then why do you often treat His speech—the Quran—as if it is silent and irrelevant to your daily decisions? When will you start listening to Him as One who speaks?
?
The passage argues that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker, especially when one has the power to speak. In your life, when you have the power to speak truth against injustice or to give advice, do you choose silence? What does your silence say about your pursuit of perfection?
?
The prophets' consensus that God speaks is verified by miracles. Do you recognize the 'miracles' of divine speech in your own life? Or do you demand more signs while ignoring the clear words of the Quran? How will you respond today to God's speech?

The Eternal Nature of God's Speech and Refutation of Anthropomorphism
Explains that God's speech is an eternal attribute expressed in scriptures, and refutes those who literalize it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq, God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, expressed in scriptures, and why the views of those who literalize it as sounds are untenable.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of sounds or letters.

In this passage, the author clarifies the true meaning of God's speech, distinguishing between the eternal attribute and its temporal expressions in scripture. He presents the orthodox Sunni position and then refutes the anthropomorphic views of the Hanabila and Hashwiyya who attribute literal sounds to God. Understanding this distinction is crucial to preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Notice the key terms like 'كلام نفسي' and how the author contrasts different views.
The passage begins by stating the disagreement is about the meaning and pre-eternity of God's speech.
2
Identify the Ahl al-Ḥaqq position
Find the description of God's speech as an eternal attribute not of sounds or letters, and how it is indicated by expression, writing, gesture.
The author uses the analogy of referring to God in different languages to show the expressions differ but the referent is the same.
3
Understand the opposing views
The passage mentions that all sects oppose this, claiming speech is only audible letters. Then the Hanabila and Hashwiyya go further, saying those letters and sounds are pre-eternal.
The passage ends with an example of their absurdity: some claim the book's binding is pre-eternal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for tawhid. If God's speech were made of sounds, it would be temporal and contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الخلاف في معنى كلامه، وفي قدمه وحدوثه ، فعند أهل الحق كلامه ليس من جنس الأصوات والحروف. بل صفة أزلية قائمة بذات الله تعالى منافيه للسكوت ، والآفة كما في الخرس والطفولة هو بها آمر ناه مخبر وغير ذلك، يدل عليها بالعبارة أو الكتابة أو الإشارة فإذا عبر عنها بالعربية ،فقرآن وبالسريانية ،فإنجيل، وبالعبرانية فتوراة والاختلاف على العبارات دون المسمى كما إذا ذكر الله تعالى بألسنة متعددة، ولغات مختلفة. وخالفنا في ذلك جميع الفرق. وزعموا أنه لا معنى للكلام إلا المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة الدال على المعاني المقصودة، وأن الكلام النفسي غير معقول. ثم قالت الحنابلة والحشوية: إن تلك الأصوات والحروف مع تواليها وترتب بعضها على البعض ، وكون الحرف الثاني من كل كلمة مسبوقاً بالحرف المتقدم عليه كانت ثابتة في الأزل، قائمة بذات الباري تعالى وتقدس، وأن المسموع من أصوات القراء والمرئي من أسطر الكتاب نفس كلام الله تعالى القديم، وكفى شاهداً على جهلهم ما نقل عن بعضهم أن الجلدة والغلاف أزليان، وعن بعضهم أن الجسم
And the disagreement is only concerning the meaning of His speech, and concerning its pre-eternity (qidam) and temporality (ḥudūth). For according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq (People of Truth), His speech is not of the genus of sounds and letters. Rather, it is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) subsisting in the essence of God, exalted be He, incompatible with silence and defect, as in muteness (kharas) and childhood (ṭufūla). By it He is commanding (āmir), forbidding (nāhin), informing (mukhbiʾ), and other than that. It is indicated by expression (ʿibāra), writing (kitāba), or gesture (ishāra). So if it is expressed in Arabic, it is the Qurʾān; in Syriac, it is the Injīl (Gospel); in Hebrew, it is the Tawrāt (Torah). The variation is in the expressions (ʿibārāt), not in the named thing (musammā), just as if God, exalted be He, is mentioned in multiple tongues and different languages. And in that we are opposed by all the sects. They claimed that there is no meaning of speech except that which is composed of audible letters indicating intended meanings, and that internal speech (kalām nafsī) is not rationally conceivable. Then the Ḥanābila and Ḥashwiyya said: that those sounds and letters, with their sequence, arrangement of some after others, and the fact that the second letter of every word is preceded by the preceding letter, were established in pre-eternity (azal), subsisting in the essence of the Creator, exalted and sanctified be He, and that what is heard from the voices of reciters and what is seen of the lines of the book is the very same pre-eternal speech of God, exalted be He. And sufficient as a witness to their ignorance is what is related from some of them that the binding and the cover are pre-eternal, and from some of them that the body...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and refutation passage. The author states that the real disagreement among sects is about the meaning and nature (pre-eternity vs. temporality) of God's speech. He then presents the Ahl al-Ḥaqq position: God's speech is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) subsisting in His essence, incompatible with silence and defects like muteness and childhood. Through this attribute, God commands, forbids, informs, etc. This attribute is indicated (yadullu ʿalayhā) by expression, writing, or gesture. When expressed in Arabic, it is called the Qurʾān; in Syriac, the Injīl (Gospel); in Hebrew, the Tawrāt (Torah). The differences are only in the expressions, not in the underlying meaning (musammā), similar to how God is referred to in different languages. The author then notes that all other sects oppose this view. They claim that speech is only the orderly arrangement of audible letters indicating meanings, and reject the notion of internal speech (kalām nafsī) as unintelligible. Then he specifically refutes the Ḥanābila and Ḥashwiyya, who assert that those very sounds and letters, despite their sequence and dependency, are pre-eternal (thābita fī al-azal) and subsisting in God's essence, and that the actual sounds heard from reciters and the visible script in the book are identical to God's pre-eternal speech. The author concludes with an example of their absurdity: some of them claimed that the book's binding (jild) and cover (ghilāf) are pre-eternal, and some claimed that the body [is also pre-eternal]—the sentence is cut off but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
كلام نفسي
عبارة
إنجيل
توراة
أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech is literally composed of sounds and letters, and that the Quran is the very speech of God in that literal sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not sounds or letters. The Quran is an expression of that attribute in Arabic, but the eternal speech itself is not composed of sounds.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Ḥaqq, what is the relationship between the Qurʾān, Torah, and Gospel?
A) They are different eternal speeches subsisting in God's essence.
B) They are different expressions of the same eternal divine speech.
C) They are created sounds and letters revealed in different languages.
D) They are identical in every respect, including their wording.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not temporal sounds; scriptures are expressions of that attribute, not the attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have recited the Quran countless times. Did you ever pause to consider what it means to say that the Quran is God's speech? Are you reciting the very attribute of God, or just a created expression? How does this distinction affect your reverence when you recite?
?
The Hashwiyya thought the binding and cover were eternal. What 'bindings and covers' have you mistakenly treated as eternal in your own understanding of faith? Are there aspects of your practice that you have literalized or ritualized to the point of forgetting the eternal reality behind them?
?
If God's speech is an eternal attribute, and the Quran is its expression in Arabic, what does that mean for how you approach understanding its meanings? Are you content with the outer expression, or do you seek to connect with the eternal command, prohibition, and information behind the words?

The Karramiyya Distinction Between Qawl and Kalam
Reports the Karramiyya distinction between qawl and kalam in response to a problematic view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view that God's saying (qawl) is temporal and subsists in His essence, while His speech (kalam) is His eternal power to speak.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya split God's speech into a temporal saying and an eternal power.

This passage presents the Karramiyya's response to a problem: if the recited Qur'an is eternal, how do we account for its temporal letters? They resolve it by distinguishing between God's saying (qawl), which is temporal and inheres in His essence, and His speech (kalam), which is His eternal attribute of power to speak. This allows them to affirm both eternal divine speech and the temporal nature of the recited text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the initial problem
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. Notice the first sentence describes a view that the written Qur'an became eternal after being created. This is the problem the Karramiyya address.
2
Understand the Karramiyya's reasoning
The Karramiyya choose a 'lesser evil': contradicting a proof is less severe than denying what is self-evident (the temporality of sounds). Grasp this principle.
3
Grasp the distinction between qawl and kalam
They say the composed letters are temporal and subsist in God's essence, calling them 'qawl' (saying) but not 'kalam' (speech). God's kalam is His eternal power to speak, not a sequence of sounds.
Note that qawl is said to be 'temporal without a creator' (hādith lā muḥdith).
4
Examine the rule for temporal vs. created things
The passage ends with a distinction: things with a beginning, if they subsist in the essence, are temporal by power (not created). If separate, they are created by God's word 'Be'.
This rule reinforces their view that the temporal qawl is not created by a separate act but arises from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي كتب به القرآن فانتظم حروفاً ورقوماً هو بعينه كلام الله تعالى، وقد صار قديماً، بعدما كان حادثاً، ولما رأت الكرامية أن بعض الشر أهون من البعض، وأن مخالفة الضرورة أشنع من مخالفة الدليل ذهبوا إلى أن المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى، وأنه قول الله تعالى إلا كلامه، وإنما كلامه قدرته على التكلم وهو قديم، وقوله حادث لا محدث وفرقوا بينهما، بأن كل ما له ابتداء إن كان قائماً بالذات فهو حادث بالقدرة غير محدث، وإن كان مبايناً للذات فهو محدث بقوله كن لا بالقدرة
That by which the Qur'an was written, it arranged letters and script, is the very speech of God, and it became eternal after it was created. And when the Karramiyya saw that some evil is lighter than another, and that contradicting what is self-evident is more repugnant than contradicting a proof, they went to the view that the composed [letters] of the heard sounds, its temporality subsists in God's essence, and that it is God's saying (qawl) not His speech (kalam), but His speech is His power to speak and it is eternal, and His saying is temporal without a creator, and they distinguished between them, by [saying] that everything that has a beginning, if it subsists in the essence, then it is temporal by power (qudra) not created, and if it is separate from the essence, then it is created by His word 'Be' not by power.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting a problematic view that the written Qur'an (the composed letters) is the very speech of God and that it became eternal after being created. The author then reports the Karramiyya's response. The Karramiyya, recognizing that some evil is less severe than another, and that going against what is self-evident (like the temporality of sounds) is worse than going against a proof, proposed a distinction. They said that the composed letters heard in recitation are created and subsist in God's essence—meaning they inhere in God—and they are God's saying (qawl) but not His speech (kalam). For them, God's speech (kalam) is His eternal attribute of power to speak (qudra 'ala al-takallum). His saying (qawl) is temporal but has no creator (lā muḥdith), meaning it is not created by a separate act but rather arises from the essence. They further distinguish: anything that has a beginning, if it subsists in the essence (like an attribute), then it is temporal by power (qudra) but not created (lā muḥdith). If it is separate from the essence (i.e., external), then it is created by the word 'Be' (qawluhu kun), not by power.

Key Terms
الكرامية
القول
الكلام
القدرة على التكلم
حادث لا محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya believe the Qur'an is created in the usual sense (like other creatures).
Correction: They hold the recited letters are temporal (hādith) but subsist in God's essence and are 'without a creator' (lā muḥdith), meaning they are not created by an external act but arise from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, what is God's eternal speech (kalam)?
A) The recited Qur'an
B) His power to speak
C) The written text
D) The word 'Be'

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya resolve the tension between eternal divine speech and temporal recited letters by distinguishing qawl (temporal inhering attribute) from kalam (eternal power to speak).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya chose a 'lesser evil' to avoid a greater one. Is there an area in your own life where you have knowingly settled for a lesser wrong rather than facing the full truth of God's commands? What would it cost you to embrace that truth today, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
They distinguished between qawl and kalam to preserve God's transcendence and avoid attributing temporality to His essence. In what subtle ways might you be limiting God's majesty—perhaps by reducing His attributes to fit your intellectual comfort, or by avoiding hard questions about His nature? What step can you take this week to humbly submit to His revelation even when it challenges your logic?
?
The passage states that contradicting what is self-evident is more repugnant than contradicting a proof. Have you ever ignored an obvious truth about your own sinfulness or need for repentance because it was more convenient than engaging with a doctrinal argument that would demand change? What self-evident truth about your relationship with God have you been avoiding, and how will you respond to it today?

The Mu'tazila Views on the Nature of the Qur'an
Details the Mu'tazila positions of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i on the Qur'an's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Mu'tazila theories on the Qur'an's temporality and how they differ on its location and endurance.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila agreed the Qur'an is created but differed on whether it endures or is momentary.

This passage reveals that even within the Mu'tazila, there was no single view on the Qur'an's nature. While they all rejected the eternity of the Qur'an, they differed on what exactly 'created speech' means and where it resides. Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i represent two distinct camps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note that the Mu'tazila start from a shared premise.
2
Identify the two views
Locate the views of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i. Notice how they differ on the genus of the Qur'an and whether it endures.
Abu Hashim's view is that the Qur'an is only in recitation; al-Jubba'i says it endures in inscriptions.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these differences matter: how each view affects the status of written copies of the Qur'an.
This connects to later debates about the mushaf and the Preserved Tablet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لما قطعوا بأنه المنتظم من الحروف، وأنه حادث، والحادث لا يقوم بذات الله تعالى. ذهبوا إلى أن معنى كونه متكلماً أنه خلق الكلام في بعض الأجسام، واحترز بعضهم عن إطلاق لفظ المخلوق عليه لما فيه من إيهام الخلق والافتراء، وجوزه الجمهور ثم المختار عندهم، وهو مذهب أبي هاشم ومن تبعه من المتأخرين أنه من جنس الأصوات والحروف ولا يحتمل البقاء حتى إن ما خلق مرقوماً في اللوح المحفوظ أو كتب في المصحف لا يكون قرآناً، وإنما القرآن ما قرأه القارىء، وخلقه الباري من الأصوات المتقطعة والحروف المنتظمة، وذهب الجبائي إلى أنه من جنس غير الحروف تسمع عند سماع الأصوات، وتوجد بنظم الحروف، وبكتبها، ويبقى عند المكتوب والحفظ، ويقوم باللوح المحفوظ وبكل مصحف وكل لسان، ومع هذا فهو واحد لا يزداد بازدياد المصاحف، ولا ينقص بنقصانها، ولا يبطل ببطلانها.
And the Mu'tazila, when they were certain that the composed [text] is of letters and that it is temporal, and that the temporal does not subsist in God's essence, they went to the view that the meaning of His being 'speaker' is that He created speech in some bodies. And some of them refrained from applying the term 'created' to it because of the suggestion of falsehood and slander, but the majority permitted it. Then the chosen view according to them—and it is the school of Abu Hashim and those who followed him among the later scholars—is that it is of the genus of sounds and letters and does not admit of permanence, such that what is created as inscribed in the Preserved Tablet or written in the mushaf is not the Qur'an; rather the Qur'an is what the reader recites, and the Creator creates it from intermittent sounds and ordered letters. And al-Jubba'i went to the view that it is of a genus other than letters, which is heard when sounds are heard, and it exists through the order of letters and their writing, and it remains with the written and memorized [text], and it subsists in the Preserved Tablet and in every mushaf and every tongue, and yet it is one, not increasing with the increase of mushafs, nor decreasing with their decrease, nor invalidated by their invalidation.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage details the Mu'tazila positions on the createdness of the Qur'an. The Mu'tazila agreed that the composed Qur'an is of letters and temporal, and that the temporal cannot subsist in God's essence. Therefore, they said that God's being 'speaker' means He creates speech in some body (e.g., the air or the angel Gabriel). Some Mu'tazila avoided calling the Qur'an 'created' (makhluq) because it might imply falsehood, but the majority allowed it. Then the author presents the two main views within the Mu'tazila. The first, attributed to Abu Hashim and his followers, is that the Qur'an is of the genus of sounds and letters and does not endure. Thus, what is inscribed in the Preserved Tablet or written in the mushaf is not actually the Qur'an; the Qur'an is only what the reader recites at the moment, created by God from intermittent sounds and ordered letters. The second view, held by al-Jubba'i, is that the Qur'an is of a genus other than letters—something that is heard when sounds are heard and exists through the order of letters and writing. This entity endures and subsists in the Preserved Tablet, in every mushaf, and in every tongue. Despite being in many places, it remains one, not increasing or decreasing with the number of copies, and not becoming invalid when they are destroyed.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أبو هاشم
الجبائي
خلق الكلام
اللوح المحفوظ
المصحف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila all agree that the Qur'an is a non-enduring event.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i held that the Qur'an endures and subsists in written and memorized forms, unlike Abu Hashim's view that it is only momentary in recitation.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim's view, where is the Qur'an truly located?
A) In the Preserved Tablet
B) In the mushaf
C) In the recitation of the reader
D) In every tongue

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agreed the Qur'an is created but disagreed on its ontological status—whether it is a non-enduring event (Abu Hashim) or an enduring entity (al-Jubba'i).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila debated whether the Qur'an is a momentary event or an enduring entity. When you listen to or recite the Qur'an, do you sense it as a living, present word that transcends time, or as a text from the past? How would your reverence change if you were fully convinced that the very same Qur'an that was revealed to the Prophet (peace be upon him) subsists in your recitation today?
?
Al-Jubba'i argued that the Qur'an remains one despite being in countless copies and tongues. Yet we often treat copies carelessly or prioritize convenience over respect. How many times have you placed a mushaf in a disrespectful position? What will you do today to honor the unity and permanence of the Qur'an in every form?
?
Abu Hashim believed the Qur'an is only real when recited, not when written. Do you treat the written Qur'an as merely ink on paper, or as a manifestation of the eternal word? What can you do to ensure that every copy you own is approached with the same awe as if you were hearing it fresh from the Creator?

The Two Syllogisms on the Qur'an's Eternity and Temporality
Presents two syllogisms and schools' responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two syllogisms that lead to contradictory conclusions about the Qur'an's eternity and temporality, and how each theological school responded by denying a premise.

Core Idea
Two syllogisms force a choice: the Qur'an is eternal or temporal—each school denies a premise to avoid contradiction.

The debate over the Qur'an's nature is not arbitrary; it rests on two rigorous syllogisms using definitive premises. One concludes the Qur'an is eternal (as a divine attribute), the other that it is temporal (as composed of sounds). Since both cannot be true, each school had to reject one premise. This lesson unpacks those syllogisms and the strategies employed by the Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, Ash'aris, and Hashwiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two syllogisms
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two arguments and the schools' reactions.
Notice the symmetry: one syllogism uses 'attributes are eternal', the other 'sounds are temporal'.
2
Identify each school's rejected premise
List which premise each group denied: Mu'tazila denied the Qur'an is an attribute; Karramiyya denied all attributes are eternal; Ash'aris denied it is from sounds/letters; Hashwiyya denied the composed text is temporal.
The passage dismisses Hashwiyya and Karramiyya as irrelevant.
3
Understand why only Ash'aris and Mu'tazila remain
The Ash'ari position (eternal internal speech) and Mu'tazila position (temporal created speech) are the meaningful contenders.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on internal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنه انتظم من المقدمات القطعية والمشهورة قياسان ينتج أحدهما: قدم كلام الله تعالى. وهو أنه من صفات الله تعالى، وهي قديمة. والآخر حدوثه، وهو أنه من جنس الأصوات وهي حادثة، فاضطر القوم إلى القدح في أحد القياسين، ومنع بعض المقدمات ضرورة امتناع حقية النقيضين، فمنعت المعتزلة كونه من صفات الله تعالى والكرامية كون كل صفة قديمة والأشاعرة كونه من جنس الأصوات والحروف، والحشوية كون المنتظم من الحروف حادثاً، ولا عبرة بكلام الحشوية والكرامية، فبقي النزاع بيننا وبين المعتزلة، وهو في التحقيق عائد إلى إثبات كلام النفس، ونفيه وأن القرآن هو المتلو هذا المؤلف من الحروف الذي هو كلام حسي، وإلا فلا نزاع لنا في حدوث كلام الحس ولا لهم في قدم النفس لو ثبت وعلى البحث والمناظرة في ثبوت الكلام النفسي، وأنه هو القرآن ينبغي أن يحمل ما نقل عن مناظرة أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله ستة أشهر، ثم اشتهر رأيهما على أن من قال بخلق القرآن فهو كافر.
And the conclusion is that from definitive and well-known premises two syllogisms are composed, one of which yields the eternity of God's speech: that it is from God's attributes, and they are eternal. And the other yields its temporality: that it is from the genus of sounds, and they are temporal. So the people were forced to disparage one of the two syllogisms and deny some premises, due to the impossibility of both contradictories being true. Thus the Mu'tazila denied that it is from God's attributes; the Karramiyya denied that every attribute is eternal; the Ash'aris denied that it is from the genus of sounds and letters; and the Hashwiyya denied that the composed [text] of letters is temporal. And no regard is given to the speech of the Hashwiyya and Karramiyya, so the dispute remains between us and the Mu'tazila, and it is in reality returning to affirming internal speech (kalam nafs) or denying it, and that the Qur'an is what is recited, this composed [text] of letters which is sensory speech. Otherwise, there is no dispute for us about the temporality of sensory speech, nor for them about the eternity of internal speech if it were established. And upon the investigation and debate about the establishment of internal speech, and that it is the Qur'an, one should interpret what was narrated from the debate of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) for six months, then their view became famous that whoever says the Qur'an is created is a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the summary of the entire discussion. The author states that from definitive and well-known premises, two syllogisms can be constructed: one proving the eternity of God's speech (since it is an attribute of God, and attributes of God are eternal), and the other proving its temporality (since it is of the genus of sounds, which are temporal). Because both conclusions cannot be true, each theological school was forced to attack one of the syllogisms and deny some premise. The Mu'tazila denied that the Qur'an is a divine attribute; the Karramiyya denied that every divine attribute is eternal (they said some, like qawl, are temporal); the Ash'aris denied that the Qur'an is from the genus of sounds and letters (since they affirm an eternal internal speech); and the Hashwiyya denied that the composed letters are temporal (they said the written text became eternal). The author dismisses the Hashwiyya and Karramiyya as irrelevant, leaving the real debate between the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila. That debate, in reality, reduces to the affirmation or denial of internal speech (kalam nafsi). Both sides agree that the recited Qur'an (sensory speech of sounds and letters) is temporal. The disagreement is about whether there is an eternal, internal attribute of speech in God that corresponds to the recited Qur'an. If internal speech is established, then the object of the dispute shifts. Finally, the author addresses the famous ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that whoever says the Qur'an is created is a disbeliever. This ruling should be interpreted in light of the debate on internal speech: namely, if one denies internal speech and says the Qur'an is entirely created sensory speech, that is disbelief. So the ruling is understood to apply to those who reject the eternal attribute of speech.

Key Terms
قدم كلام الله
حدوثه
الحشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris deny that the recited Qur'an is temporal.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that the recited Qur'an (sounds/letters) is temporal; they assert an eternal internal speech beyond the sensory expression.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group denied that every attribute of God is eternal?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Ash'aris
C) Karramiyya
D) Hashwiyya

Key Takeaway
Two contradictory syllogisms force each school to deny a premise, leaving the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila as the main contenders.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an in prayer, do you feel that you are uttering something that has existed with Allah from eternity, or something that came into being? How does your answer affect the reverence you feel for each word?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that the Qur'an is a divine attribute because they saw it as created. In your own approach to the Qur'an, do you treat it as a mere book of guidance, or do you honour it as an inseparable part of Allah's essence? What change would acknowledging its eternity demand from you today?
?
This lesson shows that each school had to sacrifice a premise to avoid logical contradiction. Examine your own beliefs: is there any assumption you hold about the Qur'an that you have never tested with reason? Will you now re-evaluate that assumption, even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Real Debate: Internal Speech and Abu Hanifa's Ruling
Identifies the real dispute as internal speech and interprets Abu Hanifa's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the core dispute between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila is over the existence of internal speech (kalam nafsi), and how Abu Hanifa's ruling should be interpreted in light of this debate.

Core Idea
The real debate is about internal speech—a non-sensory, eternal attribute of God—and Abu Hanifa's ruling targets its deniers.

Once the weaker positions are set aside, the real debate is between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. At stake is whether God has an eternal, internal speech that underlies the temporal recited Qur'an. The passage also clarifies a famous ruling by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, which is often misunderstood. This lesson explains the central issue and how to properly understand that ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on internal speech and Abu Hanifa
Focus on how the author shifts from the syllogisms to the real dispute and the interpretation of Abu Hanifa's statement.
Look for the phrase 'وهو في التحقيق عائد إلى إثبات كلام النفس'.
2
Define internal speech (kalam nafsi)
Internal speech is an eternal, non-sensory attribute of God that is the meaning expressed by the recited Qur'an.
Sensory speech (kalam hissi) is the temporal sounds/letters we recite.
3
Understand the agreement on sensory speech
Both sides agree that the recited (sensory) Qur'an is temporal. The dispute is whether there is an eternal internal speech.
The author says: 'فلا نزاع لنا في حدوث كلام الحس ولا لهم في قدم النفس لو ثبت'.
4
Interpret Abu Hanifa's ruling
The ruling 'whoever says the Qur'an is created is a disbeliever' must be understood as referring to those who deny internal speech entirely, not those who affirm internal speech but accept sensory temporality.
Abu Hanifa's debate with Abu Yusuf was about the createdness of the sensory Qur'an, but their final position was that denying the eternal attribute of speech constitutes disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
Abu Hanifa's ruling was aimed at those who deny God's eternal speech. If you were to be asked today, would you affirm that the Qur'an is the uncreated speech of God, even if its recited form is temporal? Are you willing to accept that His speech is not like our speech?

Defining Mutakallim and the First Proof for Eternal Speech
Taftazani defines mutakallim and defends eternal internal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of mutakallim leads to affirming eternal internal speech in God.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'speaker' is one in whom speech subsists, not one who creates external speech.

This lesson presents Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani's first argument for the eternity of God's speech. He begins by clarifying what it means for God to be a speaker, which is a key theological concept. The argument hinges on the distinction between internal meaning and external composition.

Guided Path
1
Read and Understand the Definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Taftazani defines mutakallim: it is one in whom speech subsists (qāma bihi al-kalām).
This is a technical definition, different from the common understanding of 'speaker'.
2
Identify the Core Argument
The author argues that composite speech (letters) is originated (ḥādith) and cannot subsist in God, so the only option is that God's speech is a non-composite meaning (al-maʿnā).
This is a classic kalam argument using the division of necessary, impossible, and possible.
3
Consider the Objection
An objector raises two points: (1) 'speaker' may be used even when speech is not subsisting, and (2) composition might be instantaneous, like in a memory or stamp impression, so it could be eternal.
The objection tries to undermine the necessity of the definition and the impossibility of eternal composition.
4
Examine Taftazani's Response
The author responds that subsistence does not require permanence or simultaneous parts, and 'speaking by another's tongue' is a metaphor. He also distinguishes instantaneous imagined composition from actual ordered composition.
This clarifies the conditions for speech subsisting in a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا : الاستدلال على قدم الكلام إن معنى المتكلم من قام به الكلام، والمنتظم من الحروف حادث يمتنع قيامه بذات الله تعالى فتعين المعنى إذ لا ثالث . فإن قيل قد يطلق المتكلم ولا بقاء للكلام ليقوم ولو سلم فبلسانه لا به بل بلسان غيره، والنظم قد يكون دفعي الأجزاء كما في نفس الحافظ ونقش الطابع فلا يمتنع قدمه وقيامه بالذات، قلنا لا يشترط في القيام البقاء ولا التلبس بجميع الأجزاء والتكلم بلسان الغير مجاز عن إلقاء الكلام إليه ، وكونه النظم مرتب الأجزاء ممتنع البقاء ضروري وهو غير الصورة المرسومة أو المرقومة منه .
He said to us: The evidence for the eternity of speech is that the meaning of 'speaker' (mutakallim) is one in whom speech subsists. The composition of letters is originated; it is impossible for it to subsist in the essence of God, so the meaning [i.e., internal speech] is necessarily affirmed, since there is no third option. For it could be said: The term 'speaker' may be applied [even] when there is no speech subsisting, and if conceded, it is by his tongue, not by him, but rather by another's tongue, and composition may be instantaneous in parts, as in the mind of the memorizer and the impression of a stamp, so its eternality and subsistence in the essence are not impossible. We say: Subsistence does not require permanence nor the simultaneous presence of all parts. Speaking by another's tongue is a metaphor for casting speech to him. And the composition that is arranged in parts is necessarily impossible to be permanent, and it is different from the image drawn or written from it.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the eternity of God's speech and defends it against objections. It begins by defining 'speaker' (mutakallim) as one in whom speech subsists, not as one who creates speech externally. Composite speech (letters) is temporal and cannot be an attribute of God, so the speech that subsists in God must be a non-composite meaning (kalam nafsī), as there is no third option. The author then raises an objection: the term 'speaker' might be used even when speech is not subsisting in the speaker, e.g., when one speaks through another's tongue, or if the speech is instantaneous (like a mental recitation). The author answers: subsistence does not require that speech be permanent or have all parts present simultaneously. Speaking through another's tongue is a metaphorical expression for causing speech to occur in another. A composition arranged in successive parts cannot be eternal because it would require temporal sequence; this is different from an instantaneous mental image or impression.

Key Terms
المتكلم (mutakallim)
قيام (subsistence)
المعنى (meaning/speech)
النظم (composition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a speaker must always have speech present at the moment of speaking, and that instantaneous composition could be eternal.
Correction: Taftazani explains that subsistence does not require permanence (baqā') nor simultaneous presence of all parts, so speech can be eternal while not being constantly 'present' in a temporal sense. Instantaneous composition that is arranged in parts cannot be eternal because it would require temporal order.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer to the objection that speech may be instantaneous (daf'i al-ajzā')?
A) Instantaneous speech is impossible for God.
B) Subsistence does not require permanence or all parts existing at once.
C) The objection is invalid because the objector has no basis.
D) The speech of God is not composite.

Key Takeaway
A speaker is defined by having speech subsist in them, and God's eternal internal speech is necessary because external composed speech cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage defines mutakallim as one in whom speech subsists. In your daily life, what does it mean for you to have speech 'subsist' in you? Are your words merely fleeting, or do they carry an enduring reality that reflects your inner self?
?
If God's eternal internal speech is a single meaning, how does that reshape your relationship with the Quran? Will you now approach its words as expressions of that one divine meaning rather than as a created book?

Second Proof: The Speaker Is One in Whom Speech Subsists
Author begins a second proof that mutakallim is one with speech, not its creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that God's speech is internal (kalam nafsī) and not sensory, based on the definition of 'speaker'.

Core Idea
A speaker is one in whom speech subsists, not one who creates sounds.

This lesson presents the second proof from the author for the eternity and internality of God's speech. It begins by defining the term 'mutakallim' (speaker) and uses an analogy with motion to show that causing speech does not make one a speaker. The passage then argues that sensory speech is temporal and sequential, thus cannot be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the argument structure.
The passage begins a two-part proof; this is the first part of the second proof.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that a speaker is one in whom speech subsists, not one who originates speech elsewhere.
3
Understand the analogy
The author uses the example of motion: one who creates motion in a body is not called 'moving' (mutaharrik). Similarly, one who creates sounds is not called 'speaker'.
This analogy is central to the proof.
4
Consider the implication for God's speech
Since God creates sounds, but is not thereby called 'speaker' in the sensory sense, His speech must be internal.
The passage then argues that sensory speech cannot be God's speech because it is temporal.
5
Reflect on the incomplete nature
The passage cuts off; the full proof continues. What can you infer so far?
The author uses the temporal nature of letters to show they are created, not eternal.

قوله لنا : استدل على قدم كلام الله تعالى وكونه نفسياً لا حسياً بوجهين : أحدهما : أن المتكلم من قام به الكلام لا من أوجد الكلام، ولو في محل آخر، آخر لا يسمى متحركاً، وأن الله تعالى لا للقطع بأنه موجد الحركة في جسم بخلق الأصوات مصوتاً ، وأنا إذا سمعنا قائلاً يقول : أنا قائم نسميه متكلماً ، وإن لم نعلم أنه الموجد لهذا الكلام. بل وإن علمنا أن موجده هو الله تعالى كما هو رأي أهل الحق وحينئذ فالكلام القائم بذات الباري تعالى لا يجوز أن يكون هو الحس أعني المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة ، لأنه حادث ،ضرورة أن له ابتداء وانتهاء ، وأن الحرف الثاني من كل كلمة مسبوق بالأول مشروط بانقضائه، وأنه يمتنع اجتماع أجزائه في الوجود، وبقي شيء منها بعد الحصول على ما سبق لنا نبذ من ذلك في
He said to us: He adduced evidence for the eternity of God's speech and that it is internal, not sensory, in two ways. One of them: that the speaker (mutakallim) is one in whom speech subsists, not one who brings speech into existence, even if in another locus; the latter is not called 'mover' (mutaharrik). And that God, may He be exalted, is not—for certainty that He is the bringer into existence of motion in a body by creating sounds as a sound-maker. And I, when we hear a speaker saying 'I am standing,' we call him a speaker, even if we do not know that he is the one who produced this speech. Rather, even if we know that its originator is God—as is the view of the people of truth—then the speech subsisting in the essence of God cannot be the sensory, i.e., the composition of audible letters, because it is originated, necessarily, since it has a beginning and an end, and the second letter of every word is preceded by the first and conditioned upon its passing away, and it is impossible for its parts to coexist in existence, and something of it remains after occurrence according to what we have previously mentioned a part of that in
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage begins a second proof for the eternity of God's speech and its nature as internal (nafsī) rather than sensory. It starts by stating that a speaker is one in whom speech subsists, not one who merely creates speech elsewhere. It uses an analogy: one who creates motion in a body is not called 'moving' (mutaharrik) in the sense of having the motion in himself. Similarly, even if God creates sounds, He is not called 'speaker' in the sensory sense. The proof then argues that when we hear someone speak, we call him a speaker regardless of whether he produced the sounds or not; even if we know that God created the sounds (as per Ash'ari occasionalism), the speaker is still the one in whom the speech subsists. Therefore, God's speech must be internal, not the sensory composition of letters, because sensory speech is temporal—it has a beginning and end, each letter follows the previous and requires its cessation, and its parts cannot coexist. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, indicating that the argument continues in a later section.

Misconception: God is called speaker because He creates audible sounds.
Correction: God is called speaker because speech subsists in His essence, not because He creates sounds.

The First Argument for the Pre-Eternity of Divine Speech
Defines 'mutakallim' and argues for pre-eternal meaning speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech, which defines 'mutakallim' as one in whom speech subsists and addresses key objections regarding composition and speech through another.

Core Idea
The divine speaker is defined by speech subsisting in him, not by creating speech.

This passage presents the foundational argument for why Allah's speech must be eternal and not composed of letters. It begins by clarifying what it truly means to be a 'speaker' and then anticipates and refutes objections that might arise from examples of human speech. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on the attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'mutakallim'
The passage first states that a speaker is one in whom speech subsists. This is the key premise.
2
Trace the reasoning from temporal letters to eternal meaning
Since letter-based speech is temporal, it cannot subsist in God, so the speech must be a non-composite meaning.
3
Recognize the objection about speaker without subsisting speech
An objection is raised: sometimes 'speaker' is used even when speech does not subsist, e.g., speaking through another's tongue.
4
Explore the objection about instantaneous composition
Another objection: composition can be instantaneous, like in retention or stamp, so a composed speech could be eternal.
5
Analyze the responses to these objections
The author refutes both: subsistence does not require permanence, and composition of ordered parts is temporal by nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا : الاستدلال على قدم الكلام إن معنى المتكلم من قام به الكلام، والمنتظم من الحروف حادث يمتنع قيامه بذات الله تعالى فتعين المعنى إذ لا ثالث . فإن قيل قد يطلق المتكلم ولا بقاء للكلام ليقوم ولو سلم فبلسانه لا به بل بلسان غيره، والنظم قد يكون دفعي الأجزاء كما في نفس الحافظ ونقش الطابع فلا يمتنع قدمه وقيامه بالذات، قلنا لا يشترط في القيام البقاء ولا التلبس بجميع الأجزاء والتكلم بلسان الغير مجاز عن إلقاء الكلام إليه ، وكونه النظم مرتب الأجزاء ممتنع البقاء ضروري وهو غير الصورة المرسومة أو المرقومة منه
He said to us: The inference for the pre-eternity of speech: Indeed the meaning of 'speaker' (mutakallim) is the one in whom speech subsists, and the [speech] composed of letters is temporal (hadith), it is impossible for it to subsist in the essence of God Almighty, so the meaning (ma'na) is determined, for there is no third. For if it is said: 'Speaker' may be applied without the speech subsisting, and if granted, then by his tongue not by himself, rather by the tongue of another, and the composition (nazm) may be instantaneous (duf'i) in parts, as in the mind of the memorizer and the impression of the stamp, so its pre-eternity and subsistence in the essence are not impossible, we say: Subsistence does not require continuity nor being clothed with all the parts, and speaking by the tongue of another is a metaphor for casting speech to it, and its being a composition of ordered parts is necessarily impossible of endurance, and it is other than the form drawn or impressed.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the initial argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech. It first defines 'mutakallim' as the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it. The speech composed of letters is temporal, so it cannot subsist in God's essence; therefore, the speech must be a meaning (ma'na) without letters. An objection is then raised: the term 'speaker' is sometimes applied even when speech does not subsist, as when one speaks through another's tongue, and composition can be instantaneous (like in the mind of a memorizer or a stamp impression), so a composition of letters could be eternal and subsist in God. The response answers that subsistence does not require continuous endurance or being clothed with all parts, and speaking through another's tongue is metaphorical. The composition of ordered parts necessarily has sequence and cannot persist, and it is not the same as the mental image or impression.

الكلام
المعنى
الحروف
القدم
النظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a 'speaker' is one who creates speech, not one in whom speech subsists.
Correction: The passage defines 'mutakallim' strictly as the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who produces it elsewhere.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first argument concludes that divine speech is an eternal meaning, not composed speech.

The First Proof for Divine Speech as a Meaning
First proof: defines mutakallim and argues divine speech is mental and pre-eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that divine speech is an eternal mental attribute (nafsi) and not temporal sensible letters, based on the definition of 'mutakallim'.

Core Idea
The speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it.

This lesson presents a key argument in Islamic theology regarding the nature of divine speech. The proof establishes that God's speech must be a pre-eternal meaning inherent in His essence, rather than created letters or sounds. By examining the definition of 'speaker' (mutakallim), the argument shows that temporal speech cannot be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the definition of 'mutakallim'
Note that 'mutakallim' is defined by inherence of speech, not by creating it. Consider the analogy with motion: the mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who causes motion.
This is the foundational principle of the proof.
3
Analyze the example of the human speaker
Examine the case where we call a human speaker 'mutakallim' even if God creates their words. This reinforces that the attribute of speech inheres in the speaker, not the creator.
4
Grasp why audible speech is temporal
Notice the arguments: letters have a beginning and end, each letter is preceded by the previous one, parts cannot coexist. These show that audible speech is temporal (hadith).
Temporal things cannot subsist in God's essence.
5
Conclude that divine speech is a meaning and pre-eternal
Since sensible speech cannot be in God, the only remaining possibility is that divine speech is a meaning (ma'na) inherent in His essence, and it must be pre-eternal as an attribute of God.
This completes the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لنا : استدل على قدم كلام الله تعالى وكونه نفسياً لا حسياً بوجهين : أحدهما : أن المتكلم من قام به الكلام لا من أوجد الكلام، ولو في محل آخر، آخر لا يسمى متحركاً، وأن الله تعالى لا للقطع بأنه موجد الحركة في جسم بخلق الأصوات مصوتاً ، وأنا إذا سمعنا قائلاً يقول : أنا قائم نسميه متكلماً ، وإن لم نعلم أنه الموجد لهذا الكلام. بل وإن علمنا أن موجده هو الله تعالى كما هو رأي أهل الحق وحينئذ فالكلام القائم بذات الباري تعالى لا يجوز أن يكون هو الحس أعني المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة ، لأنه حادث ،ضرورة أن له ابتداء وانتهاء ، وأن الحرف الثاني من كل كلمة مسبوق بالأول مشروط بانقضائه، وأنه يمتنع اجتماع أجزائه في الوجود، وبقي شيء منها بعد الحصول على ما سبق لنا نبذ من ذلك في بحث الكم. والحادث يمتنع قيامه بذات الباري تعالى لما سبق، فتعين أن يكون هو المعنى ، إذ لا ثالث يطلق عليه اسم الكلام، وأن يكون قديماً لما عرفت
His statement to us: He inferred the pre-eternity of the speech of God Almighty and that it is mental (nafsi) not sensible (hissi) in two ways: One of them: That the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who brings speech into existence, even if in another locus; otherwise the one who brings about motion in a body by creating sounds would not be called a mover. And we, when we hear a speaker saying 'I am standing', we call him a speaker, even if we do not know that he is the one who brought this speech into existence. Rather, even if we know that its originator is God Almighty, as is the view of the people of truth. Then the speech subsisting in the essence of the Creator cannot be the sensible speech, i.e., the composition of audible letters, because it is temporal, necessarily having a beginning and an end, and because the second letter of every word is preceded by the first and conditioned by its passing away, and because it is impossible for its parts to coexist in existence, and something of it remains after its occurrence, as we have previously touched upon in the discussion of quantity. And the temporal is impossible to subsist in the essence of the Creator, as previously established. So it is determined that it is the meaning, since there is no third thing to which the name 'speech' is applied, and that it is pre-eternal, as you have known.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs for the pre-eternity of divine speech and that it is mental (nafsi) rather than sensible (hissi). The proof begins by reiterating that 'speaker' (mutakallim) is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it. If it were the latter, then anyone who creates speech elsewhere would be called a speaker, just as a mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who causes motion in another. The proof then uses an example: when we hear someone say 'I am standing', we call him a speaker even if we don't know the origin of the speech; even if we know God is the creator of that speech (as per Ash'ari doctrine), we still call the human speaker a speaker. Therefore, the speech subsisting in God's essence cannot be the audible composition of letters (sensible speech) because that is temporal—it has a beginning and end, each letter is sequential, parts cannot coexist, and something remains after its occurrence. Since the temporal cannot subsist in God's essence, the speech must be a meaning (ma'na) that is pre-eternal. This establishes that divine speech is an eternal attribute, not created letters.

الكلام
النفسي
الحسي
الحادث
المعنى
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'speaker' is defined by the ability to create speech, not by inherence.
Correction: The passage argues that a speaker is one in whom speech subsists, not one who brings speech into existence. Otherwise, anyone who creates motion would be called a mover, which is not the case.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for mental speech shows that God's speech is an eternal meaning inherent in His essence, not temporal letters.

Divine Speech as an Eternal Meaning
Definition and defense of divine speech as eternal meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is an eternal meaning subsisting in God's essence and how it answers objections from the Mu'tazila and Hanabila.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal meaning, not composed of letters.

This passage presents a foundational Ash'ari argument about the nature of divine speech. After establishing that divine speech cannot be an accident, the author defines it as an eternal meaning subsisting in God's essence. He then anticipates and refutes two major objections: one from the Mu'tazila concerning the definition of a speaker, and another from the Hanabila concerning the possibility of non-sequential speech. This lesson guides you through the core argument and its defenses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial position
Read the opening statement where the author concludes that divine speech must be an eternal meaning because accidents cannot subsist in God.
This sets the stage for the entire discussion.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the objection that 'speaker' would not apply to sensory speech, and the examples (prince speaking with vizier's tongue, jinn speaking with epileptic's tongue).
They argue that speech subsists only in the tongue, not the essence.
3
Examine the Hanabila objection
Understand their claim that composed letters can be non-sequential (instantaneous), as in memory or a stamp, so the Qur'an could be eternal as both arrangement and meaning.
They argue that sequential order is due to physical limitations, not essential.
4
Study the unified response
See how the author refutes both objections: for the Mu'tazila, the active participle indicates occurrence, not permanence; for the Hanabila, speech concerns audible letters, and a letter in God's essence is not intelligible.
This reinforces the original position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث الكلام. والحادث يمتنع قيامه بذات الباري تعالى لما سبق، فتعين أن يكون هو المعنى، إذ لا ثالث يطلق عليه اسم الكلام، وأن يكون قديماً لما عرفت، فإن اعترض من قبل المعتزلة أنه لو كان المتكلم من قام به الكلام، لما صح إطلاقه حقيقة على المتكلم بالكلام الحسي، لأنه لا بقاء له ولا اجتماع لأجزائه، حتى يقوم بشيء. ولو سلم، فإنما يقوم بلسانه لا بذاته، وأيضاً لما صح قولهم: الأمير يتكلم بلسان الوزير والجني يتكلم بلسان المصروع. ومن قبل الحنابلة أن المنتظم من الحروف قد لا يكون مترتب الأجزاء بل دفعياً كالقائم بنفس الحافظ، وكالحاصل على الورقة من طابع فيه نقش الكلام. وإنما لزوم الترتيب في التلفظ والقراءة لعدم مساعدة الآلة. فالقرآن الذي هو اسم للنظم والمعنى جميعاً لا يمتنع أن يكون قديماً قائماً بذات الباري تعالى. أجيب بأن كون المتكلم من قام به الكلام ثابت عرفاً ولغة، وكون المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة مترتب الأجزاء ممتنع البقاء ثابت ضرورة، وما ذكرتم سنداً لمنعها تمويه. أما الأول: فلأن المعتبر في اسم الفاعل وجوب المعنى لا بقاؤه سيما في الأعراض السيالة، كالمتحرك والمتكلم. ولو سلم فيكفي التلبس ببعض أجزائه ولا يشترط القيام بكل جزء من أجزاء المحل كالسامع والباصر والذائق وغير ذلك، ومعنى التكلم بلسان الغير إلقاء الكلام إليه مجازاً. وأما الثاني فلأن الكلام في المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة لا في الصورة المرسومة في الخيال، أو المخزونة في الحافظة، أو المنقوشة بأشكال الكتابة على أن قيام الحرف والصوت بذات الله تعالى ليس بمعقول. وإن كانت غير مترتب الأجزاء لحرف واحد مثلاً.
Discussion of speech. The accident cannot subsist in the essence of God Most High, as previously established, so it is determined that it is the meaning, for there is no third thing to which the name 'speech' is applied, and that it is eternal, as you have learned. If it is objected from the Mu'tazila that if the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, then it would not be correct to apply it truly to the speaker of sensory speech, because it has no permanence nor conjunction of its parts, such that it subsists in something. And even if conceded, it subsists only in his tongue, not in his essence; and also their saying would not be correct: 'The prince speaks with the tongue of the vizier' and 'the jinn speaks with the tongue of the epileptic.' And from the Hanabila: that the composition of letters may not be sequential in parts but instantaneous, like what subsists in the memory itself, and like what appears on paper from a stamp with an imprint of speech. The necessity of order in utterance and reading is only due to the inability of the instrument. So the Qur'an, which is a name for both the arrangement and the meaning, is not prevented from being eternal, subsisting in the essence of God Most High. The answer: That the speaker is one in whom speech subsists is established customarily and linguistically, and that the composition of audible letters is sequential in parts and impossible of permanence is established necessarily, and what you mentioned as a proof for preventing it is sophistry. As for the first [objection]: because the consideration in the active participle is the occurrence of the meaning, not its permanence, especially in transient accidents, such as 'moving' and 'speaking'. And even if conceded, it suffices to be adorned with some of its parts, and subsistence in every part of the locus is not required, as in 'hearing', 'seeing', 'tasting', and others. And the meaning of 'speaking with the tongue of another' is metaphorically conveying speech to him. As for the second [objection]: because speech concerns the composition of audible letters, not the form depicted in imagination, or stored in memory, or engraved in shapes of writing; furthermore, the subsistence of the letter and sound in the essence of God Most High is not intelligible, even if they are non-sequential in parts, like a single letter for example.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is an eternal meaning subsisting in God's essence. The author first states the position: since an accident cannot subsist in God (as previously argued), the only possibility is that speech is a meaning (ma'na), eternal, and there is no third category. Then he presents two objections. The first is from the Mu'tazila: if 'speaker' means one in whom speech subsists, then it would not apply to someone speaking sensory speech, which has no permanence and is sequential; and even if conceded, it would only subsist in the tongue, not the essence; also phrases like 'the prince speaks with the tongue of the vizier' would be problematic. The second objection is from the Hanabila: composed letters can be non-sequential (instantaneous), as in the case of the memorizer or a printed stamp, so sequential order is not necessary; thus the Qur'an, as both arrangement and meaning, could be eternal. The author then provides a two-part answer. For the first objection: the active participle indicates the occurrence of the meaning, not its permanence, especially for transient accidents like movement; furthermore, it suffices to be adorned with some parts, not all, as in hearing, seeing, etc. The phrase 'speaking with another's tongue' is metaphorical. For the second objection: the discussion concerns audible, composed letters, not mental images or written shapes; moreover, the subsistence of a letter or sound in God's essence is not intelligible, even if it were a single letter. Thus both objections are refuted, affirming that divine speech is an eternal meaning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that divine speech must be composed of letters and sounds like human speech.
Correction: The author shows that divine speech is an eternal meaning, not composed of letters. Letters are accidents that cannot subsist in God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can divine speech not be an accident (hadith)?
A) Because accidents require a body to subsist in, and God is not a body.
B) Because God's speech is eternal and accidents are not.
C) Because the Mu'tazila proved it is not an accident.
D) Because the Hanabila argued it is composed of letters.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal meaning subsisting in God's essence; objections based on sensory speech or letter composition are refuted by distinguishing between meaning and letters.
