The Elaborated Proof and Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's Answer
Sanusi gives a detailed version of the fourth proof and Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's answer, ending with an unanswered counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed version of the fourth proof, Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's answer that God's speech in pre-eternity is not specifically any type, and the unresolved counter-objection about genus and species.

Core Idea
God's speech in pre-eternity is a genus without species until creation gives it specific form.

The fourth proof is restated in detail, listing the types of speech (command, prohibition, etc.) and arguing that if eternal, each would lack its required correlate. Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan answers that in pre-eternity the speech is not any specific type. The passage ends with a counter-objection: a genus without species is unintelligible, and change in the eternal is impossible. This lesson explores that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the elaborated proof
Read the longer version: 'His speech includes command, prohibition, informing, questioning, calling... if eternal, would necessitate command without one commanded, etc., all foolishness.'
This expands the proof by listing multiple types of speech.
2
Study Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's answer
His answer: in pre-eternity, it is not a command, nor a prohibition, nor news; it becomes one of these divisions only in the future.
This answer denies that the speech in eternity has any specific type.
3
Analyze the counter-objection
The objection: a genus (like speech) without any of its species (command, etc.) is not intelligible. Also, change in the eternal is impossible.
This challenges the coherence of Ibn Sa'id's answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الرابع قال: الرابع. إن الأمر والنهي والخبر حيث لا مخاطب ولا سامع سفه وعبث وأجيب بأن كلامه إنما يصير أحد الأقسام فيما لا يزال ولو سلم ففي الكلام النفسي يكفي مجرد مخاطب معقول، وإلى هذا يؤول ما قاله الجمهور إن المعدوم مأمور على تقدير الوجود، فالأمر الأزلي اقتضاء ممن سيكون كطلب التعلم من ابن سيولد، وكأوامر النبي لمن يوجد، وأيضاً السفه أن يخلو عن الحكمة ما يتعلق بها، والقديم ليس كذلك ولو سلم فيكفي وجود الحكمة ولو بعد حين. قوله الرابع: تقديره أن كلامه يشتمل على أمر ونهي وإخبار واستخبار ونداء وغير ذلك، فلو كان أزلياً لزم الأمر بلا مأمور والنهي بلا منهي، والإخبار بلا سامع والنداء والاستخبار بلا مخاطب وكل ذلك سفه وعبث لا يجوز أن ينسب إلى الحكيم تعالى وتقدس. وأجيب بوجوه: أحدها لعبد الله بن سعيد القطان وهو أن كلامه في الأزل ليس بأمر ولا نهي ولا خبر ذلك وإنما يصير أحد الأقسام فيما لا يزال. فإن قيل: وجود الجنس من غير أن يكون أحد الأنواع ليس بمعقول، وأيضاً التغيير على القديم محال.
The Fourth Proof. He said: The fourth: That command, prohibition, and news where there is no addressee nor hearer is foolishness and absurdity. And it was answered that His speech becomes one of the divisions only in what is yet to be (future), and even if it is granted, in internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) a mere rational addressee suffices. And to this reduces what the majority said: that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is commanded on the condition of existence, so the eternal command is a requisition from one who will be, like the request for learning from a son who will be born, and like the commands of the Prophet to those who will exist. Also, foolishness is that something lacks wisdom that pertains to it, and the Eternal is not like that, and even if granted, the existence of wisdom at a later time suffices. His statement: The fourth: Its interpretation is that His speech includes command, prohibition, informing, questioning, calling, and other things. So if it were eternal, it would necessitate command without one commanded, prohibition without one prohibited, informing without a hearer, calling and questioning without an addressee. All of that is foolishness and absurdity that cannot be attributed to the Wise, Exalted and Sanctified. And it was answered with several ways: One of them is by Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, that His speech in pre-eternity is not a command, nor a prohibition, nor news of that; it only becomes one of the divisions in what is yet to be. Then if it is said: The existence of the genus without any of its species is not intelligible, and also change (al-taghayyur) in the eternal is impossible.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-dalil al-rabi') against the doctrine that God's speech (kalam) is eternal, along with answers to it. The proof is stated briefly: if God's speech consists of commands, prohibitions, and reports, and if it were eternal (azali), then in pre-eternity there would be no creatures to address, making it foolish (safah) and absurd (abath) to attribute such speech to the Wise God. The answer given is that eternal speech in itself is not actually a command, prohibition, or report; it only becomes so in relation to future existents (fima la yazal). It also appeals to the concept of 'internal speech' (kalam nafsi) which requires only a rational addressee, not an actual one. The majority view is invoked: the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is considered commanded on the condition of existence. Then a more detailed version of the proof is presented (qawluhu: al-rabi'...): God's eternal speech would have to include various types (command, prohibition, etc.), each requiring a subject. If no such subject exists eternally, the speech is absurd. The answer from Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan is that in pre-eternity, God's speech is not specifically any of these types; it only becomes one of them temporally. The passage ends with a counter-objection: if the speech is not any specific type, then it cannot even exist as a genus (since a genus without species is unintelligible), and change in the eternal is impossible. This counter-objection is left unanswered in the provided text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sa'id's answer resolves all issues about eternal speech.
Correction: The counter-objection shows that saying speech is not specifically any type raises new problems about genus and change.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the counter-objection at the end of the passage?
A) That Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's answer is correct and final.
B) That eternal speech cannot be a genus without being a species, and change is impossible in the eternal.
C) That the fourth proof is valid and cannot be answered.
D) That God's speech is created in time.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's answer that eternal speech is not specifically any type invites counter-arguments about the coherence of a genus without species and the impossibility of change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God’s commands as if they are only relevant when you feel like obeying, forgetting that His word is eternal and stands even when no one listens? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence for His speech beyond your immediate circumstances?
?
The counter-objection about change in the eternal is a real intellectual challenge. When you encounter a theological puzzle that has no easy answer, do you grow impatient or do you learn to trust God’s wisdom even in mystery? Will you commit to sitting with such questions rather than dismissing them?
?
If God’s speech is a genus that only becomes specific when related to creation, then the Quran you read is a temporal expression of an eternal attribute. Does that change the way you approach every verse, knowing it flows from God’s timeless being? What is one specific change you will make in your recitation or reflection today?

Eternal Divine Speech: Objection and Five Responses
Al-Taftazani presents the objection and five responses defending eternal divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians defended the eternity of divine speech against the objection that commands, prohibitions, and information require an addressee, and the five key responses they formulated.

Core Idea
Eternal divine speech is defended by five sophisticated responses to the addressee objection.

This passage presents a central objection to the eternity of divine speech: if God's speech is eternal, it includes commands, prohibitions, and information without an addressee, which seems foolish. Al-Taftazani then recounts five responses from scholars, each offering a different angle to resolve the difficulty. As you read, notice how each response preserves divine speech's eternity while explaining its relation to temporally existing beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
The objection argues that eternal commands without addressees would be foolish. Understand the logical structure.
2
Examine the first response (al-Qattan)
This response denies that divine speech is actually command/prohibition in eternity; it becomes so only in time.
3
Examine the second response (mental speech)
This response distinguishes sensory speech (needs addressee) from mental speech (needs only rational addressee).
4
Examine the third, fourth, and fifth responses
The third is the majority view: addressing the non-existent on assumption of existence; the fourth and fifth define foolishness in terms of wisdom.
The fourth response notes the Eternal does not need a purpose; the fifth sees wisdom in future outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الرابع
قال: الرابع. إن الأمر والنهي والخبر حيث لا مخاطب ولا سامع سفه وعبث وأجيب بأن كلامه إنما يصير أحد الأقسام فيما لا يزال ولو سلم ففي الكلام النفسي يكفي مجرد مخاطب معقول، وإلى هذا يؤول ما قاله الجمهور إن المعدوم مأمور على تقدير الوجود، فالأمر الأزلي اقتضاء ممن سيكون كطلب التعلم من ابن سيولد، وكأوامر النبي ﷺ لمن يوجد، وأيضاً السفه أن يخلو عن الحكمة ما يتعلق بها، والقديم ليس كذلك ولو سلم فيكفي وجود الحكمة ولو بعد حين.
قوله - الرابع : تقديره أن كلامه يشتمل على أمر ونهي وإخبار واستخبار ونداء وغير ذلك، فلو كان أزلياً لزم الأمر بلا مأمور والنهي بلا منهي، والإخبار بلا سامع والنداء والاستخبار بلا مخاطب وكل ذلك سفه وعبث لا يجوز أن ينسب إلى الحكيم تعالى وتقدس.
وأجيب بوجوه: أحدها لعبد الله بن سعيد القطان وهو أن كلامه في الأزل ليس بأمر ولا نهي ولا خبر وإنما يصير أحد الأقسام فيما لا يزال. فإن قيل: وجود الجنس من غير أن يكون أحد الأنواع ليس بمعقول، وأيضاً التغيير على القديم محال. قلنا: هو أراد أنه أمر واحد يعرف له التنوع بحسب التعلقات الحادثة من غير أن يتغير في نفسه.
وثانيها: أن وجود المخاطب إنما يلزم في الكلام الحسي. وأما النفسي فيكفيه وجوده العقلي.
وثالثها: أن السفه والعبث إنما يلزم لو خوطب المعدوم وأمر في عدمه، وأما على تقدير وجوده بأن يكون طلباً للفعل ممن سيكون فلا كما في طلب الرجل تعلم ولده الذي أخبره صادق بأنه سيولد وكما في خطاب النبي ﷺ بأوامره ونواهيه مع كل مكلف يولد إلى يوم القيامة، إذ اختصاص خطاباته بأهل عصره وثبوت الحكم فيمن عداه بطريق القياس بعيد جداً. نعم لو قيل خطاب الحاضرين قصداً والغائبين والمعدومين ضمناً وتبعاً ليس من السفه في شيء لكان شيئاً. واعلم أن هذا الجواب هو المشهور من الجمهور، وكلامهم متردد في أن معناه أن المعدوم مأمور في الأزل بأن يتمثل ويأتي بالفعل على تقدير الوجود، أو المعدوم ليس بمأمور في الأزل لكن لما استمر الأمر الأزلي إلى زمان وجوده صار بعد الوجود مأموراً.
ورابعها: أن السفه هو أن يخلو عن الحكمة والعاقبة الحميدة ما يتعلق بها، والقديم ليس كذلك، إذ لا يطلب لثبوته حكمة وغرض.
وخامسها: أن السفه هو الخالي عن الحكمة بالكلية، والأمر الأزلي ليس كذلك لترتب الحكمة عليه فيما لا يزال.
The Fourth Proof. He said: The fourth. Indeed, command, prohibition, and information where there is no addressee nor hearer are foolishness and futility. And it was answered that His speech only becomes one of the divisions in what will be, and even if conceded, in mental speech a merely rational addressee suffices. And to this reduces what the majority said: that the non-existent is commanded on the assumption of existence. So the eternal command is a requirement from one who will be, like the request for learning from a son who will be born, and like the commands of the Prophet ﷺ to those who will exist. Also, foolishness is to be devoid of wisdom that pertains to it, and the Eternal is not like that, and even if conceded, the existence of wisdom suffices even if after a time. His statement: the fourth: its meaning is that His speech includes command, prohibition, information, inquiry, call, and other things. So if it were eternal, it would necessitate command without a commanded one, prohibition without a prohibited one, information without a hearer, call and inquiry without an addressee, and all that is foolishness and futility which cannot be attributed to the Wise, Exalted and Holy. And it was answered with several aspects: One of them is from 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, which is that His speech in eternity is not command, nor prohibition, nor information; rather it becomes one of the divisions in what will be. If it is said: The existence of a genus without being any of the species is not intelligible, and also change upon the Eternal is impossible. We say: He meant that it is one command that is known to have diversity according to temporal relations without changing in itself. The second: That the existence of an addressee is only necessary in sensory speech. As for mental speech, its rational existence suffices. The third: That foolishness and futility would only follow if the non-existent was addressed and commanded in its non-existence. But on the assumption of its existence, such that it is a request for action from one who will be, then no, as in the request of a man for the learning of his son whom a truthful informant told him would be born, and as in the address of the Prophet ﷺ with his commands and prohibitions to every accountable person born until the Day of Resurrection, for restricting his addresses to his contemporaries and establishing the ruling for others by way of analogy is very far-fetched. Indeed, if it were said that addressing the present intentionally and the absent and non-existent implicitly and secondarily is not at all foolishness, that would be something. Know that this answer is the well-known one from the majority, and their speech fluctuates as to its meaning: that the non-existent is commanded in eternity to represent and perform the action on the assumption of existence, or that the non-existent is not commanded in eternity but when the eternal command continues until the time of his existence, he becomes commanded after existence. The fourth: That foolishness is to be devoid of wisdom and praiseworthy outcome pertaining to it, and the Eternal is not like that, for no wisdom or purpose is sought for His subsistence. The fifth: That foolishness is that which is devoid of wisdom entirely, and the eternal command is not like that because wisdom is arranged upon it in what will be.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against the eternity of divine speech, an objection, and five responses. The objection is that if God's speech is eternal, it would include commands, prohibitions, and information without any addressee, which would be foolishness (safah) and futility ('abath), impossible for the Wise. The first response, from 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, argues that in eternity, divine speech is not actually any of these kinds; it becomes command, etc., only in time. A sub-objection is raised: a genus without any species is unintelligible, and change in the Eternal is impossible. The response clarifies that speech is one single thing that acquires diversity by temporal relations without changing in itself. The second response distinguishes sensory speech (which requires an actual addressee) from mental speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), which only requires a rational addressee. The third response, the well-known one from the majority, argues that addressing the non-existent on the assumption of existence (i.e., as a request for action from one who will be) is not futile, analogous to a man requesting learning from a son predicted to be born, or the Prophet's commands to all future believers. The text notes ambiguity in the majority's position: whether the non-existent is literally commanded in eternity or becomes commanded when the eternal command reaches him. The fourth response defines foolishness as being devoid of wisdom; the Eternal is not like that because no purpose is sought for His own being. The fifth response defines foolishness as entirely devoid of wisdom; the eternal command has wisdom arranged upon it in the future.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الخبر
الكلام النفسي
السفه
العبث
الحكمة
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that divine speech must be temporal.
Correction: The responses show that eternal speech is compatible with temporal events; the objection fails because it does not consider mental speech, future-oriented commands, or relational categories.

Checkpoint
According to the third response (the majority view), why is addressing the non-existent not considered foolishness?
A) Because the non-existent can hear speech.
B) Because the command is given on the assumption of future existence, like requesting learning from a son who will be born.
C) Because God only addresses angels, not humans.
D) Because foolishness is only related to physical actions.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of divine speech is defended by five responses that explain how commands, prohibitions, and information can exist without immediate addressees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objection that an eternal command without an addressee is 'foolishness and futility', did any part of you secretly agree? Are there commands of Allah in your life whose wisdom you doubt because you see no immediate recipient or purpose? How will the fifth response—that wisdom is arranged 'in what will be'—change your patience with His decrees?
?
The third response uses the example of a man asking his future son to learn. Think of a specific command from Allah that you find difficult to obey today. Do you believe that command was addressed to you from eternity? If so, does that give you a sense of being personally called by name before time—and how does that impact the urgency with which you respond?
?
The fourth response defines foolishness as being devoid of wisdom 'pertaining to it'. Reflect on a trial or blessing in your life that seemed pointless at first. In light of this lesson, are you willing to trust that Allah’s eternal speech concerning your life is never futile, even when you cannot see the wisdom? What would it look like for you to live today with that conviction?

Refuting Mu'tazilite Arguments on Eternal Speech
Taftazani refutes three Mu'tazilite arguments against the eternity of divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes three Mu'tazilite arguments against the eternity of divine command by distinguishing between eternal speech and its temporal relations.

Core Idea
Eternal speech has temporal attachments; differentiation preserves logical consistency.

The Mu'tazila argued that eternal command implies perpetual obligation and equal relation to all acts, leading to absurdities. Taftazani shows how the eternal attribute of speech relates to created acts through temporal attachments (ʿalāqāt ḥāditha) by God's will, resolving the contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the three Mu'tazilite proofs
Identify the fifth, sixth, and seventh arguments as presented by Taftazani. Note how each relies on the assumption that eternal attributes cannot have temporal limitations.
These proofs challenge the eternity of command based on its supposed implications.
2
Understand the unified answer
Notice how Taftazani responds by distinguishing eternal speech from its temporal relations (taʿalluqāt). These relations are created and specific to particular times and individuals.
The key is that the attribute is eternal but its connections to events are temporal.
3
See the application to the seventh proof
The same logic applies: the eternal attribute's relation is not equal across time, because the attachments are initiated by God's will. This avoids the contradiction of command and prohibition applying to the same act.
4
Reflect on the 'ilzāmī' nature
Understand that the seventh proof is presented as an implicative argument against Ashʿarīs, who deny objective moral values, but the answer shows it fails because of temporal attachments.
This highlights the difference between Ashʿarī and Muʿtazilī ethical frameworks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : بقية الأدلة لو كان أزلياً لكان أبدياً فيبقى التكليف في دار الجزاء : السادس : يكون مكالمة موسى عليه السلام أبداً لا في الطور وحده . السابع : يستوي نسبته إلى المتعلقات فيكون المأمور منهياً وبالعكس. قلنا : التعلق حادث بالاختيار. ثم قال : الوجه الخامس والسادس : من تمسكات المعتزلة أن الأمر لو كان أزلياً لكان التكليف باقياً أبداً حتى في دار الجزاء لأن ما يثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه ولما اختص مكالمة موسى عليه السلام بالطور، بل استمر أزلاً وأبداً واللازم باطل إجماعاً. وجوابهما : أن الكلام وإن كان أزلياً لكن تعلقاته بالأشخاص والأفعال حادثة بإرادة من الله تعالى واختيار فيتعلق الأمر بصلاة زيد مثلاً بعد بلوغه، وينقطع عند موته، ويتعلق الكلام بموسى عليه السلام في الطور، على أنك إذا تحققت فالمختص بالطور سماع الكلام وظهوره، وبهذا يخرج الجواب عن الوجه السابع وهو أن القديم يستوي نسبته إلى جميع ما يصح تعلقه به كما في العلم والقدرة، فيتعلق الأمر والنهي بكل فعل حتى يكون المأمور منهياً وبالعكس، واللازم باطل قطعاً، وهذا إلزامي علينا حيث لا يقول بالحسن والقبح لذات الفعل ليمنع صحة تعلق الأمر بما يتعلق به النهي وبالعكس.
Fifth: The remaining proofs: If it were eternal, it would be everlasting, so obligation would remain in the abode of recompense. Sixth: The conversation of Moses (peace be upon him) would be eternal, not only on the mountain. Seventh: Its relation to all things would be equal, so the commanded would be prohibited and vice versa. We said: The relation is temporally originated by choice. Then he said: The fifth and sixth aspect: From the arguments of the Mu'tazila is that if the command were eternal, obligation would remain forever even in the abode of recompense, because what is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible; and the conversation of Moses (peace be upon him) would not be specific to the mountain, but would continue eternally and everlastingly, and the consequent is false by consensus. And the answer to both: That speech, even if eternal, its relations to persons and actions are temporally originated by the will of God Most High and His choice. So the command attaches, for example, to Zayd's prayer after his maturity, and ceases at his death; and speech attaches to Moses (peace be upon him) on the mountain, except that if you realize, what is specific to the mountain is the hearing of speech and its manifestation. And with this emerges the answer to the seventh aspect, which is that the eternal has equal relation to all that is possible to relate to it, as in knowledge and power. So the command and prohibition would attach to every act, such that the commanded would be prohibited and vice versa; and the consequent is absolutely false. And this is an implicative argument against us, since they do not affirm goodness and badness in the essence of the act to prevent the soundness of the command attaching to what the prohibition attaches to and vice versa.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents three objections from the Mu'tazila against the eternity of God's command and speech, and provides a unified response. The author first lists the fifth, sixth, and seventh proofs succinctly. The fifth: if the command were eternal, obligation would persist even in the afterlife because eternal things cannot cease. The sixth: God's speech to Moses would be eternal, not limited to the mountain. The seventh: an eternal attribute relates equally to all things, so command and prohibition would apply to every act, causing contradiction. Then the author gives the answer. For the fifth and sixth: although speech (kalām) is eternal, its attachments (taʿalluqāt) to persons and actions are temporally originated (ḥāditha) by God's will and choice. The command to pray attaches to Zayd only after he reaches maturity and ends at his death. Similarly, God's speech to Moses was on the mountain, but the speech itself is not confined to that time; rather, it was the hearing and manifestation that were specific. The same logic answers the seventh proof: the differentiation of attachments prevents the equal relation that the Mu'tazila assumed. The author adds that this objection is an 'ilzāmī' (implicative) argument against the Ash'aris because the Ash'aris do not hold to objective ethical valuation (ḥusn wa qubḥ dhātī), which would otherwise prevent the contradiction by denying that command and prohibition can attach to the same act based on their essences. In other words, the Mu'tazila are cornering the Ash'aris by assuming that since Ash'aris deny objective moral value, they cannot avoid the absurd conclusion, but the Ash'ari response is that the temporal relation of the command prevents the problem.

Key Terms

التعلق
التكليف
دار الجزاء
الإرادة
الاختيار
الحسن والقبح
الإلزامي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's eternal command means all commands are eternally applicable to every person at all times without temporal restrictions.
Correction: Taftazani explains that the eternal attribute of speech has temporal attachments (taʿalluqāt) that are created by God's will, so commands apply only at specific times to specific individuals.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani answer the seventh Mu'tazilite proof regarding the equal relation of an eternal attribute?
A) By denying that the attribute is eternal
B) By affirming that the relations are temporally originated, preventing equal relation to all acts
C) By arguing that God does not issue both command and prohibition for the same act
D) By accepting the proof but limiting it to acts that are inherently good or evil

Key Takeaway
The eternity of divine speech does not entail eternal applicability of commands; temporal attachments resolve the Mu'tazilite objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's command to you is eternal in source yet precisely timed for your life. Are you treating His commands as eternal obligations that demand immediate action, or do you postpone them as if they were optional? What would change today if you truly believed each command is personally tailored for your exact moment?
?
The Mu'tazila thought eternal speech would force perpetual obligation. But you know that God's commands cease at death. If you were to die tonight, which command would you regret not having responded to? Why are you delaying it?
?
The Ash'ari response shows that God's eternal attribute does not bind Him to timeless uniformity—He commands and prohibits based on His wise will. Does your obedience reflect trust in His timing, or do you demand that God's commands fit your own schedule and preferences? Renounce one area where you have been resisting His timing.

The Unity of God's Eternal Speech
Taftazani presents the correct Ash'ari view on the unity of God's eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God's eternal speech is one in essence yet becomes multiple through its relations, and why this view is preferred over alternatives.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one eternal word, yet it commands, prohibits, and informs without multiplicity in essence.

This passage concludes the discussion on divine speech in Sharh al-Maqasid. Taftazani presents the correct Ash'ari position that God's eternal speech is one, but becomes multiple according to its relations or effects. He refutes two alternative views—those of Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi—and affirms that the unity of speech is established by revelation and consensus, not by pure reason. This lesson clarifies the delicate balance between unity and diversity in God's attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
The passage is dense, so reading it twice may help.
2
Identify the three views on divine speech
Find the views of the Ash'aris, Ibn Sa'id, and al-Razi. Understand how each defines the nature of God's speech in eternity.
The correct view is the one Taftazani endorses.
3
Examine the proof from consensus and analogy
Notice the appeal to consensus (ijma') and the analogy with knowledge and power to show that one attribute can relate to many things.
This is a key argument for unity despite apparent multiplicity.
4
Reflect on the limitation of human intellect
Taftazani concludes that even if we cannot fully grasp how speech is one yet diverse, we accept it by revelation.
This humility is characteristic of Ash'ari theology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة. خاتمة المذهب أن كلامه الأزلي واحد يتكثر بحسب التعلق لا على أنه إنما يتكثر فيما لا يزال كما زعم ابن سعيد، ولا على أنه خبر ومرجع البواقي إليه كما زعم الإمام الرازي بل على أنه إنما ثبت بالسمع ولم يرد بالتعدد، ولم يمتنع التكلم بالأمر والنهي والخبر وغيرها بكلام واحد كما في العلم والقدرة. قال عبد الله بن سعيد: إنه في الأزل ليس شيئاً من الأقسام، وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال وقد عرفت ضعفه. وقال الإمام الرازي: هو في الأزل خبر، ومرجع البواقي إليه، لأن الأمر بالشيء إخبار باستحقاق فاعله الثواب وتاركه العقاب والنهي بالعكس، وعلى هذا القياس وضعفه ظاهر، لأن ذلك لازم الأمر والنهي لا حقيقتهما. والأقرب ما ذكره إمام الحرمين، وهو أن ثبوت الكلام إنما هو بالسمع، دون العقل، ولم يرد بالتعدد، بل انعقد الإجماع على نفي كلام فان[؟] قديم ولم يمتنع التكلم بالأمر والنهي والخبر وغيرها بكلام واحد. فحكمنا بأنه واحد يتعلق بجميع التعلقات كما في سائر الصفات، وإن كانت العقول قاصرة عن إدراك كنه هذا المعنى، وإذا تحققت فالأمر كذلك في الذات وجميع الصفات، وقد يستدل على وحدة الكلام بأنه لو تعدد لم ينحصر في عدد لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع الأعداد على السواء، وقد مر ذلك في القدرة.
He said: Conclusion. The conclusion of the doctrine is that His eternal speech is one, becoming multiple according to relation, not that it becomes multiple only in what is to come as Ibn Saʿīd claimed, nor that it is a report and the rest refer back to it as Imam al-Rāzī claimed. Rather, it is established by revelation, and there is no transmitted evidence for multiplicity, and it is not impossible to speak with command, prohibition, report, and others with one speech, as in knowledge and power. ʿAbdullāh ibn Saʿīd said: In eternity it is not any of the divisions, but becomes one of them in what is to come—and you have already recognized its weakness. Imam al-Rāzī said: It is in eternity a report, and the rest refer back to it, because commanding something is informing that its doer deserves reward and its abandoner punishment, and prohibition is the opposite, and by this analogy; but its weakness is apparent, because that is a necessary consequence of command and prohibition, not their essence. The closest view is what Imām al-Ḥaramayn mentioned, which is that the affirmation of speech is only by revelation, not by reason, and there is no transmitted evidence for multiplicity; rather, consensus has occurred on negating a perishable [؟] ancient speech, and it is not impossible to speak with command, prohibition, report, and others with one speech. So we have judged that it is one, relating to all relations as in the rest of the attributes, even if the intellects are incapable of perceiving the reality of this meaning. And if you realize, the matter is likewise in the essence and all attributes. And one may argue for the unity of speech by saying that if it were multiple, it would not be confined to a number, because the relation of the Efficient Cause to all numbers is equal, and this has already been mentioned regarding power.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) on the nature of God's speech. The correct doctrine is that God's eternal speech is one (wāḥid) but becomes multiple (yatakaththar) according to its relation (biḥasab al-taʿalluq). It is not that it becomes multiple only in the future (as claimed by Ibn Saʿīd), nor that it is in eternity only a report (khabar) and the other types (command, prohibition) return to it (as claimed by Imam al-Rāzī). Rather, the reality of speech is established by revelation (samʿ), not by reason, and there is no transmitted evidence for multiplicity. The consensus (ijmāʿ) is that speech is one, and it is not impossible that command, prohibition, report, etc. are all expressed by one speech, just as with knowledge and power. The view of ʿAbdullāh ibn Saʿīd is that in eternity it is none of the types, and becomes one of them later; this is weak. Imam al-Rāzī's view is that it is in eternity a report, and the rest are reducible to it, because commanding something is informing that its doer deserves reward and its abandoner punishment; but this is a description of the consequence of command, not its essence. The closer view is that of Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī): the affirmation of speech is by revelation, not reason, and there is no transmitted evidence for multiplicity, and consensus is on negating a perishable (?) ancient speech, and it is not impossible to speak with command, prohibition, report, and others with one speech. So the Ash'aris judge that it is one, relating to all relations like other attributes, even if intellects are incapable of perceiving the reality of this. The same applies to the essence and all attributes. One proof for unity: if speech were multiple, it would not be limited to a number because the relation of the Agent (al-mūjib) to all numbers is equal, as was mentioned regarding power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unity of divine speech means it cannot be described as command, prohibition, and report simultaneously.
Correction: The Ash'ari view is that speech is one in essence but becomes multiple through its relations, so it can encompass all types without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the correct Ash'ari view on divine speech according to this conclusion?
A) It becomes multiple only in the future
B) It is a report in eternity and the rest refer back to it
C) It is one eternal speech that diversifies by relation without being multiple in itself
D) It is multiple in eternity by division

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that God's eternal speech is one in essence, diverse in relation, and this is known by revelation and consensus, not by pure reason.

?
The passage states that intellects are incapable of fully perceiving this reality. Are you willing to submit to revealed truth even when your mind cannot fully grasp it? What specific area of your faith do you struggle to accept because it defies your reasoning, and how will you actively surrender that to God this week?

God's Speech is One Eternal Attribute
Conclusion affirming oneness of divine speech and refuting alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the orthodox Ash'ari doctrine that God's speech is one eternal attribute, while refuting the views of Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi.

Core Idea
God's speech is one, relating to all commands and prohibitions without multiplicity.

In this concluding passage, the author summarizes the correct position on divine speech. He rejects two incorrect views—that speech becomes multiple in the future or that it is essentially news—and affirms that it is one eternal attribute known by revelation, capable of encompassing all types of speech through different relations. This doctrine parallels God's other attributes like knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Read the opening statement: 'The doctrine is that His eternal speech is one, multiplying according to relation.' This is the core claim.
Note that 'multiplying according to relation' means the attribute itself is one but relates to many objects.
2
Examine the refuted views
Find the two alternative views: Ibn Sa'id's claim that speech becomes multiple in the future, and al-Razi's claim that it is essentially news. Understand why each is rejected.
The author calls Ibn Sa'id's view weak, and al-Razi's view confuses consequence with reality.
3
Understand the correct position
Learn from Imam al-Haramayn that divine speech is established by revelation, not reason; no multiplicity is reported; and it is not impossible for one speech to be command, prohibition, news, etc.
This is the Ash'ari position: speech is one attribute, even if human intellect cannot fully grasp it.
4
Reflect on the additional argument
Consider the argument that if speech were multiple in essence, it would be infinite because God's relation to all numbers is equal.
This parallels an earlier argument in the discussion of divine power.

قال: خاتمة المذهب أن كلامه الأزلي واحد يتكثر بحسب التعلق لا على أنه إنما يتكثر بحسب التعلق لا على أنه إنما يتكثر فيما لا يزال كما زعم ابن سعيد، ولا على أنه خبر ومرجع البواقي إليه كما زعم الإمام الرازي بل على أنه إنما ثبت بالسمع ولم يرد بالتعدد، ولم يمتنع التكلم بالأمر والنهي والخبر وغيرها بكلام واحد كما في العلم والقدرة. قال : خاتمة المذهب أن كلامه الأزلي واحد قال عبد الله بن سعيد: إنه في الأزل ليس شيئاً من الأقسام، وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال وقد عرفت ضعفه . وقال الإمام الرازي: هو في الأزل خبر، ومرجع البواقي إليه، لأن الأمر بالشيء إخبار باستحقاق فاعله الثواب وتاركه العقاب والنهي بالعكس، وعلى هذا القياس وضعفه ظاهر، لأن ذلك لازم الأمر والنهي لا حقيقتهما والأقرب ما ذكره إمام الحرمين، وهو أن ثبوت الكلام إنما هو بالسمع، دون العقل، ولم يرد بالتعدد، بل انعقد الإجماع على نفي كلام فان قديم ولم يمتنع التكلم بالأمر والنهي والخبر غيرها بكلام واحد . فحكمنا بأنه واحد يتعلق بجميع التعلقات كما في سائر الصفات، وإن كانت العقول قاصرة عن إدراك كنه هذا المعنى، وإذا تحققت فالأمر كذلك في الذات وجميع الصفات، وقد يستدل على وحدة الكلام بأنه لو تعدد لم ينحصر في عدد لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع الأعداد على السواء، وقد مر ذلك في القدرة.
He said: Conclusion: The doctrine is that His eternal speech is one, multiplying according to relation, not that it multiplies according to relation, not that it only multiplies in the future as Ibn Sa'id claimed, nor that it is news and the reference of the rest to it as Imam al-Razi claimed, but rather that it is established only by revelation, and multiplicity has not been reported, and it is not impossible to speak with command, prohibition, news, and others with one speech, as in knowledge and power. He said: Conclusion: The doctrine is that His eternal speech is one. 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id said: In eternity it is not any of the divisions, but it becomes one of them in the future, and you have known its weakness. Imam al-Razi said: In eternity it is news, and the rest refer to it, because commanding something is informing that its doer deserves reward and its abandoner punishment, and prohibition is the opposite, and by this analogy, and its weakness is apparent, because that is a necessary consequence of command and prohibition, not their reality. The closest is what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned, which is that the affirmation of speech is only by revelation, not reason, and multiplicity has not been reported, but consensus has been established on negating perishable speech, and it is not impossible to speak with command, prohibition, news, and others with one speech. So we ruled that it is one, relating to all relations, as in the other attributes, even if intellects are incapable of comprehending the essence of this meaning. And if you realize, the matter is likewise regarding the essence and all attributes. And it may be argued for the oneness of speech that if it were multiple, it would not be limited to a number, because the relation of the Necessary to all numbers is equal, and this has passed in [the discussion of] power.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion on the nature of God's speech (al-kalam). The author affirms that God's speech is one eternal attribute, refuting two alternative views. He begins by stating the doctrine: His speech is one, multiplying only in terms of its relations (tacalluq) to different objects, not in its essence. He rejects the view of Ibn Sa'id (a Mu'tazili?) that speech becomes multiple in the future (i.e., it is created in time). He also rejects al-Razi's view that in eternity it is essentially news (khabar) and that command and prohibition are reducible to news about reward and punishment. The author points out that this confuses the necessary consequences of command and prohibition with their true realities. He then presents the correct position as taught by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni): the existence of divine speech is known only by revelation (sam'), not by reason, and there is no report of it being multiple in essence; rather, there is consensus that God's speech is not perishable (fānin). It is not impossible for one speech to encompass command, prohibition, news, etc., just as one knowledge or power can relate to many objects. Therefore, we judge that it is one attribute relating to all objects of relation, even if human intellects cannot fully grasp this. Finally, an additional argument is given: if speech were multiple in essence, it could not be limited to a finite number because the Necessary Existent's relation to all numbers is equal; this argument was already mentioned in the discussion of power.

Misconception: God's speech is multiple in essence because it appears as command, prohibition, news, etc.
Correction: God's speech is one eternal attribute; its apparent multiplicity comes only from its relations to different objects, similar to knowledge and power.

D) It is multiple because different types require different essences.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is one eternal attribute, known only by revelation, and can be command, prohibition, news, etc., without multiplicity.

Affirming al-Baqa' and the Weakness of Denial
Author introduces the need to consider disputed attributes and affirms al-baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris affirm the attribute of al-baqa' and why the author considers the argument for denying it weak.

Core Idea
Denying an attribute without proof is weak; al-baqa' is necessary for an enduring being.

The author begins this section by addressing the broader issue of disputed attributes. He argues that simply claiming no proof for an attribute is insufficient. He then introduces the attribute of al-baqa' (continuance) and presents the Ash'ari proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the author critiques the denial of attributes without proof.
2
Understand the Ash'ari analogy
The Ash'aris argue that an enduring being without continuance is like a knower without knowledge. Reflect on why this analogy works.
The analogy shows that al-baqa' is as necessary for endurance as knowledge is for knowing.
3
Distinguish from existence
The author clarifies that al-baqa' is not identical to existence, since things exist but do not endure.
This refutes the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع في صفات اختلف فيها . إذ لا تنحصر الصفات فيما ذكر التمسك بأنه لا دليل على صفة أخرى فيجب نفيها وبأنها لو كانت لعرفت لوقوع التكليف بكمال المعرفة ضعيف فمنها البقاء أثبته الشيخ وأتباعه لأن الباقي بلا بقاء كالعالم بلا علم وليس نفس الوجود إذ قد يوجد الشيء ولا يبقى وخالفه الكثيرون لوجوه : - الأول: أن المعقول منه استمرار لوجود ومعناها الوجود من حيث انتسابه إلى الزمان الثاني . الثاني : أن البقاء بالبقاء الذي ليس نفس الذات لا يكون واجباً لذاته لا سيما إذا فسر بصفة بها الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وليس هذا من افتقار صفة إلى صفة كالإرادة إلى العلم بل من افتقار الوجود . الثالث : إما أن يحتاج البقاء إلى الذوات فيدور أو بالعكس فيكون هو الواجب لا الذات أو لا يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر بل اتفق تحققهما معاً فيتعدد الواجب مع أن استغناء الصفة عن الذات ليس بمعقول . الرابع: إما أن يكون للبقاء بقاء فيلزم التسلسل وقيام المعنى بالمعنى أولى [؟] يكون فيكون كعالم بلا علم فإن قيل بقاء البقاء نفسه قلنا : فلتكن الصفات مع الذات كذلك وقد يدفع بأنه محال لما مر [؟] بخلاف كون بقاء البقاء نفسه لكن يبقى إشكال قيام المعنى بالمعنى في بقاء الصفات ولا يندفع بما قيل نحن لا نقول الصفات باقية بل الذات باق بصفاته أو بقاؤها نفسها أو نفس بقاء الذات لعدم التغاير لأن
The Seventh Section: On Attributes Disputed. Since the attributes are not limited to what was mentioned, adherence [to the claim] that there is no proof for another attribute so it must be denied, and that if they existed they would be known due to the obligation of complete knowledge, is weak. Among them is al-baqa' (continuance). The Shaykh [Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari] and his followers affirmed it because the enduring (al-baqi) without continuance is like the knower without knowledge, and it is not existence itself since a thing may exist and not endure. Many opposed them for reasons: First, that what is understood from it is the continuation of existence, and its meaning is existence in terms of its relation to the second moment. Second, that continuance by continuance that is not the essence itself cannot be necessary in itself, especially if interpreted as an attribute by which existence [occurs] in the second moment, and this is not like the dependence of an attribute on an attribute (like will on knowledge) but rather the dependence of existence. Third, either continuance needs the essences (dhawat) leading to circularity, or the reverse making it the necessary [existent] and not the essence, or neither needs the other and they coincidentally occur together leading to multiplicity of the necessary, while the independence of the attribute from the essence is unintelligible. Fourth, either continuance has a continuance leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, and that would be like a knower without knowledge. If it is said: the continuance of continuance itself [is not problematic], we say: then let the attributes [subsist] with the essence likewise. And it may be refuted by [the argument] that it is impossible for what has preceded, unlike the case of the continuance of continuance itself, but the problem of the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning remains regarding the continuance of attributes, and it is not resolved by what is said: 'We do not say the attributes are enduring, but rather the essence endures with its attributes' or 'their endurance is itself the endurance of the essence' due to lack of distinction (nafs al-taghayur) because...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on a disputed attribute: al-baqa' (continuance/eternity). The author begins by noting that the argument that there is no proof for additional attributes so they must be denied, and that if they existed they would be known due to the obligation of complete knowledge, is weak. Then he introduces al-baqa' as one such attribute. Al-Ash'ari and his followers affirm it because the enduring being without continuance would be like a knower without knowledge, and it is not identical to existence since something may exist but not endure (i.e., perishable objects). Many opponents raise four objections: (1) Al-baqa' is just the continuation of existence, so it is redundant—its meaning is merely existence related to a second moment. (2) If al-baqa' is a distinct attribute, it would depend on itself (baqa' by baqa') and cannot be necessary in itself, especially if defined as an attribute by which existence occurs in the second moment; this dependence is not like will depending on knowledge but rather the dependence of existence itself, which is problematic. (3) Circularity: either al-baqa' depends on the essence (dhat) leading to circularity, or the essence depends on al-baqa' making it the necessary existent and not the essence, or both are independent and coincidentally occur together leading to multiplicity of the necessary, while the independence of an attribute from its essence is unintelligible. (4) Infinite regress: if al-baqa' itself requires another al-baqa', there is infinite regress and the subsistence of a meaning (ma'na) in a meaning, which is like a knower without knowledge—absurd. Then the author begins a rebuttal: if it is said that the continuance of continuance itself is not problematic (i.e., the regress is avoided by self-subsistence), then we argue that attributes could likewise subsist with the essence similarly. However, this rebuttal is then itself refuted by the claim that it is impossible for the reason mentioned earlier (presumably the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in a meaning), unlike the case of the continuance of continuance itself. But the problem of a meaning subsisting in a meaning remains for the continuance of attributes, and it is not resolved by the claim that we do not say the attributes are enduring, but rather the essence endures with its attributes, or that their endurance is itself the endurance of the essence due to lack of distinction. The passage cuts off mid-argument.

Key Terms
البقاء
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-baqa' is just another name for existence itself.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that al-baqa' is not existence itself because a thing may exist but not endure.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the argument for denying attributes without proof to be weak?
A) Because no proof can ever be found for any attribute.
B) Because the burden of proof is on the denier, not the affirmer.
C) Because the duty of perfect knowledge would require knowledge of such attributes if they existed.
D) Because the Ash'aris have already proven all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm al-baqa' as a distinct attribute because an enduring being requires it, and the denial of such attributes without proof is weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological truth that you cannot fully prove or understand, do you humbly accept it or dismiss it? How does the author's condemnation of 'weak' denial challenge your approach?
?
If God's continuance is distinct from His existence, does your trust in Him reflect that He is not just existing but enduring forever? How does that change your confidence in His promises?
?
Think of an attribute of God you have neglected or doubted. What would it cost you to affirm it fully? Are you willing to submit your intellect to revelation as the Ash'aris did with al-baqa'?

First and Second Objections Against al-Baqa'
Author lists the first two objections: redundancy and dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two objections against al-baqa': that it is merely the continuation of existence and that it leads to problematic dependence.

Core Idea
Opponents argue al-baqa' is redundant or creates an unacceptable chain of dependencies.

After the Ash'ari argument, the author lists the first two objections from opponents. The first sees al-baqa' as just existence related to time, the second points out that if it is a distinct attribute, it becomes dependent in a problematic way.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic and translation for both objections. Note how they are distinct: one about meaning, the other about dependence.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection states that al-baqa' is conceptually just the continuation of existence, i.e., existence related to the second moment. This makes it redundant with existence itself.
Consider: if al-baqa' is nothing more than existence at a later time, it is not a separate attribute.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection argues that if al-baqa' is a distinct attribute, then it must have a continuance of its own (baqa' bil-baqa'), which cannot be necessary in itself. This dependence is different from the dependence of will on knowledge; it is dependence of existence itself, which is more fundamental and problematic.
The author compares this to will depending on knowledge, but argues it's worse because it's existence depending on something.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع في صفات اختلف فيها . إذ لا تنحصر الصفات فيما ذكر التمسك بأنه لا دليل على صفة أخرى فيجب نفيها وبأنها لو كانت لعرفت لوقوع التكليف بكمال المعرفة ضعيف فمنها البقاء أثبته الشيخ وأتباعه لأن الباقي بلا بقاء كالعالم بلا علم وليس نفس الوجود إذ قد يوجد الشيء ولا يبقى وخالفه الكثيرون لوجوه : - الأول: أن المعقول منه استمرار لوجود ومعناها الوجود من حيث انتسابه إلى الزمان الثاني . الثاني : أن البقاء بالبقاء الذي ليس نفس الذات لا يكون واجباً لذاته لا سيما إذا فسر بصفة بها الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وليس هذا من افتقار صفة إلى صفة كالإرادة إلى العلم بل من افتقار الوجود . الثالث : إما أن يحتاج البقاء إلى الذوات فيدور أو بالعكس فيكون هو الواجب لا الذات أو لا يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر بل اتفق تحققهما معاً فيتعدد الواجب مع أن استغناء الصفة عن الذات ليس بمعقول . الرابع: إما أن يكون للبقاء بقاء فيلزم التسلسل وقيام المعنى بالمعنى أولى [؟] يكون فيكون كعالم بلا علم فإن قيل بقاء البقاء نفسه قلنا : فلتكن الصفات مع الذات كذلك وقد يدفع بأنه محال لما مر [؟] بخلاف كون بقاء البقاء نفسه لكن يبقى إشكال قيام المعنى بالمعنى في بقاء الصفات ولا يندفع بما قيل نحن لا نقول الصفات باقية بل الذات باق بصفاته أو بقاؤها نفسها أو نفس بقاء الذات لعدم التغاير لأن
The Seventh Section: On Attributes Disputed. Since the attributes are not limited to what was mentioned, adherence [to the claim] that there is no proof for another attribute so it must be denied, and that if they existed they would be known due to the obligation of complete knowledge, is weak. Among them is al-baqa' (continuance). The Shaykh [Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari] and his followers affirmed it because the enduring (al-baqi) without continuance is like the knower without knowledge, and it is not existence itself since a thing may exist and not endure. Many opposed them for reasons: First, that what is understood from it is the continuation of existence, and its meaning is existence in terms of its relation to the second moment. Second, that continuance by continuance that is not the essence itself cannot be necessary in itself, especially if interpreted as an attribute by which existence [occurs] in the second moment, and this is not like the dependence of an attribute on an attribute (like will on knowledge) but rather the dependence of existence. Third, either continuance needs the essences (dhawat) leading to circularity, or the reverse making it the necessary [existent] and not the essence, or neither needs the other and they coincidentally occur together leading to multiplicity of the necessary, while the independence of the attribute from the essence is unintelligible. Fourth, either continuance has a continuance leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, and that would be like a knower without knowledge. If it is said: the continuance of continuance itself [is not problematic], we say: then let the attributes [subsist] with the essence likewise. And it may be refuted by [the argument] that it is impossible for what has preceded, unlike the case of the continuance of continuance itself, but the problem of the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning remains regarding the continuance of attributes, and it is not resolved by what is said: 'We do not say the attributes are enduring, but rather the essence endures with its attributes' or 'their endurance is itself the endurance of the essence' due to lack of distinction (nafs al-taghayur) because...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on a disputed attribute: al-baqa' (continuance/eternity). The author begins by noting that the argument that there is no proof for additional attributes so they must be denied, and that if they existed they would be known due to the obligation of complete knowledge, is weak. Then he introduces al-baqa' as one such attribute. Al-Ash'ari and his followers affirm it because the enduring being without continuance would be like a knower without knowledge, and it is not identical to existence since something may exist but not endure (i.e., perishable objects). Many opponents raise four objections: (1) Al-baqa' is just the continuation of existence, so it is redundant—its meaning is merely existence related to a second moment. (2) If al-baqa' is a distinct attribute, it would depend on itself (baqa' by baqa') and cannot be necessary in itself, especially if defined as an attribute by which existence occurs in the second moment; this dependence is not like will depending on knowledge but rather the dependence of existence itself, which is problematic. (3) Circularity: either al-baqa' depends on the essence (dhat) leading to circularity, or the essence depends on al-baqa' making it the necessary existent and not the essence, or both are independent and coincidentally occur together leading to multiplicity of the necessary, while the independence of an attribute from its essence is unintelligible. (4) Infinite regress: if al-baqa' itself requires another al-baqa', there is infinite regress and the subsistence of a meaning (ma'na) in a meaning, which is like a knower without knowledge—absurd. Then the author begins a rebuttal: if it is said that the continuance of continuance itself is not problematic (i.e., the regress is avoided by self-subsistence), then we argue that attributes could likewise subsist with the essence similarly. However, this rebuttal is then itself refuted by the claim that it is impossible for the reason mentioned earlier (presumably the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in a meaning), unlike the case of the continuance of continuance itself. But the problem of a meaning subsisting in a meaning remains for the continuance of attributes, and it is not resolved by the claim that we do not say the attributes are enduring, but rather the essence endures with its attributes, or that their endurance is itself the endurance of the essence due to lack of distinction. The passage cuts off mid-argument.

Key Terms
البقاء
الوجود
الزمان الثاني
الإرادة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, how is the dependence of al-baqa' different from the dependence of will on knowledge?
A) It is the same kind of dependence.
B) It is the dependence of an attribute on another attribute.
C) It is the dependence of existence itself.
D) It is the dependence of the essence on the attribute.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues al-baqa' is redundant, while the second argues it leads to an illegitimate dependence of existence on an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical objection to a core belief (like God's everlastingness), do you tend to dismiss it or engage with it? What does this lesson's careful enumeration of objections teach you about addressing doubts honestly?
?
The second objection argues that existence itself cannot depend on an attribute. How does this challenge your understanding of God's self-sufficiency? Are you relying on rational arguments or just accepting teachings passively?
?
If someone asked you why we affirm al-baqa' as a distinct attribute despite the redundancy objection, what would you say? What steps will you take to ensure your faith is intellectually grounded?

Third and Fourth Objections Against al-Baqa'
Author lists the third and fourth objections: circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth objections: circularity between attribute and essence, and infinite regress.

Core Idea
The third objection presents three scenarios all leading to absurdity; the fourth raises the specter of infinite regress.

The next two objections are more complex. The third presents a trilemma regarding the relationship between al-baqa' and the (divine) essence. The fourth argues that if al-baqa' is an attribute, it would require its own continuance, leading to infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the third objection which presents three possibilities: al-baqa' depends on essence, essence depends on it, or both are independent. Each leads to an absurdity.
The third option implies multiplicity of necessary existents, which violates tawhid.
2
Read the fourth objection
Read the fourth objection about infinite regress. Understand why it compares to 'a knower without knowledge'.
The fourth objection uses the principle that a meaning cannot subsist in a meaning (قيام المعنى بالمعنى).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Rebuttal and the Problem of Subsistence of Meaning
Author presents a rebuttal and notes the unresolved problem of meaning subsisting in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attempted rebuttal to the fourth objection and the remaining problem of the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning.

Core Idea
The rebuttal shifts the blame, but the problem of meaning subsisting in meaning remains unresolved.

After presenting the four objections, the author records a possible rebuttal from the Ash'ari side. However, the rebuttal itself faces a difficulty: the issue of 'subsistence of a meaning in a meaning' (قيام المعنى بالمعنى) persists, and the common attempt to rephrase does not solve it.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal
Read the rebuttal starting from 'فإن قيل'. The opponent might say that the continuance of continuance itself is not problematic. The Ash'ari reply argues consistency.
2
Identify the further refutation
The author then says this reply can be refuted because the case is different, but the problem of meaning subsisting in meaning remains for attributes.
The refutation uses the principle that it is impossible for a meaning to subsist in a meaning.
3
Understand the final issue
The final sentence notes that rephrasing (e.g., 'the essence endures with its attributes') does not resolve the problem due to lack of distinction.
The passage cuts off, but the issue is left open.

?

?

Identify it now, and commit to seeking knowledge about it, even if the answer remains difficult.

The Ash'ari Proof for Permanence
Sanusi presents the Ash'ari proof for permanence as an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that permanence is an additional attribute distinct from existence, based on the necessary being's permanence.

Core Idea
Permanence is not existence itself; it is an extra meaning subsisting in the necessary being.

Before examining the objections, we need to grasp the Ash'ari reasoning for affirming permanence as a separate attribute. The argument begins with the necessity of permanence for the Necessary Being and concludes that permanence is a distinct meaning, not reducible to existence or negations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the logical flow from the premise to the conclusion.
Notice the analogy with knowledge and power.
2
Identify the claim
The author states that the Necessary Being is permanent necessarily, so there must be a meaning of permanence subsisting in Him.
3
Understand the distinctions
Permanence is not a negation or relation, nor is it identical to existence, because things can exist without being permanent (like accidents).
This shows it is an additional attribute.

فمن الصفات المختلف فيها البقاء أثبته الشيخ الأشعري وأشياعه من أهل السنة . لأن الواجب باق بالضرورة، فلا بد أن يقوم به معنى هو البقاء كما في العالم والقادر ، لأن البقاء ليس من السلوب والإضافات وهو ظاهر، وليس أيضاً عبارة عن الوجود، بل زائداً عليه لأن الشيء قد يوجد ولا يبقى كالأعراض ، سيما السيالة، وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنه ليس صفة زائدة على الوجود لوجوه : الأول: أن المعقول منه استمرار الوجود ومعناها الوجود من حيث انتسابه إلى الزمان الثاني . الثاني : أن البقاء بالبقاء الذي ليس نفس الذات لا يكون واجباً لذاته لا سيما إذا فسر بصفة بها الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وليس هذا من افتقار صفة إلى صفة كالإرادة إلى العلم بل من افتقار الوجود . الثالث : إما أن يحتاج البقاء إلى الذوات فيدور أو بالعكس فيكون هو الواجب لا الذات أو لا يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر بل اتفق تحققهما معاً فيتعدد الواجب مع أن استغناء الصفة عن الذات ليس بمعقول . الرابع: إما أن يكون للبقاء بقاء فيلزم التسلسل وقيام المعنى بالمعنى أولى يكون فيكون كعالم بلا علم فإن قيل بقاء البقاء نفسه قلنا : فلتكن الصفات مع الذات كذلك وقد يدفع بأنه محال لما مر بخلاف كون بقاء البقاء نفسه لكن يبقى إشكال قيام المعنى بالمعنى في بقاء الصفات ولا يندفع بما قيل نحن لا نقول الصفات باقية بل الذات باق بصفاته أو بقاؤها نفسها أو نفس بقاء الذات لعدم التغاير لأن الأول باطل بالضرورة، والثاني بإيجابه جواز كون بقاء الذات كذلك حتى لا يثبت قديم آخر، والثالث بامتناع قيام صفة الشيء بما ليس عينه وإن لم يكن غيره
Among the disputed attributes is permanence (al-baqa'), affirmed by al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers from Ahl al-Sunna. Because the Necessary Being is permanent necessarily, so there must subsist in Him a meaning which is permanence, as in the case of knowledge and power. For permanence is not among negations or relations, and that is clear; nor is it an expression of existence, but rather additional to it, because a thing may exist and not be permanent, like accidents, especially transitory ones. And the majority held that it is not an attribute additional to existence, for reasons: First: that what is understood from it is continuation of existence, and no meaning for that except existence in terms of its relation to the second time. Second: that permanence — by a permanence that is not the essence itself — would not be necessary in itself, especially if interpreted as an attribute by which existence occurs in the second time; and this is not dependence of an attribute on an attribute like will on knowledge, but rather dependence of existence. Third: either permanence needs essences leading to circularity, or the opposite making it the necessary being not the essence, or neither needs the other but both happen together leading to multiplicity of necessary beings, while the independence of the attribute from the essence is not intelligible. Fourth: either permanence has a permanence leading to infinite regress and subsistence of a meaning in a meaning is more appropriately impossible, so it would be like a knower without knowledge; if it is said 'the permanence of permanence itself', we say: then let the attributes with the essence be likewise; and it may be avoided by its being impossible as previously mentioned, unlike the permanence of permanence itself; but the problem of subsistence of a meaning in a meaning remains regarding the permanence of attributes, and it is not removed by what is said: we do not say the attributes are permanent, but rather the essence is permanent with its attributes, or their permanence is the same as the permanence of the essence due to lack of distinction. For the first is false necessarily, the second entails allowing the essence's permanence to be likewise so that no other eternal is established, and the third entails the impossibility of an attribute of a thing subsisting in what is not its essence even if it is not other.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the case of the attribute al-baqa' (permanence) from among the disputed attributes. The author first gives the Ash'ari proof: the Necessary Being is necessarily permanent, so there must be a meaning (ma'na) that is permanence subsisting in Him, analogous to how knowledge and power are meanings. The proof argues that permanence is not a negation or a relation, nor is it identical to existence, because something can exist without being permanent (like accidents). Then the majority (al-aktharun) argue that permanence is not an additional attribute, giving four objections. First objection: permanence is just the continuation of existence, i.e., existence in relation to a second time. Second objection: if the Necessary Being were permanent by a permanence not identical to the essence, that permanence would not be necessary in itself, especially if defined as that by which existence occurs in the second time; this is not dependence of one attribute on another but dependence of existence itself, which is impossible. Third objection: a regress argument—either permanence depends on the essence (circular), or the essence depends on permanence (making permanence the necessary being), or neither depends leading to multiple necessary beings. Fourth objection: if permanence itself had a permanence, that would lead to infinite regress and the problematic subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. The author then provides responses: the first objection is false by necessity; the second would allow that the essence's permanence could be similar, preventing the establishment of another eternal; the third is refuted by the impossibility of an attribute of a thing subsisting in what is not its essence even if not other.

Key Terms
البقاء
الشيخ الأشعري
الواجب
الصفات الزائدة
السلوب والإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think permanence is just existence continuing, so it is not an extra attribute.
Correction: The passage argues that permanence is additional to existence because something can exist without being permanent (accidents), so it cannot be identical to existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say permanence is not identical to existence?
A) Because existence is necessary for permanence
B) Because a thing may exist without being permanent, like accidents
C) Because permanence is a negation
D) Because the Necessary Being does not exist

Key Takeaway
Permanence is an additional attribute because the Necessary Being is necessarily permanent and permanence is neither existence nor a negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are surrounded by things that exist but are not permanent—your body, your possessions, your relationships. When did you last act as if they would last forever? Based on this lesson, how should your awareness of Allah's exclusive permanence change your attachment to them?
?
If permanence is an additional attribute beyond existence, then your own existence today is a gift that could vanish at any moment. What would it mean for you to live each day in the light of that reality, not just intellectually but in how you spend your time and energy?
?
The Ash‘ari proof shows that permanence is a real meaning subsisting in Allah, not mere endurance. How does knowing that Allah's permanence is a positive, active attribute change the way you seek constancy in your faith and character? What one concrete step will you take this week to embody that permanence in your devotion?

The Four Objections to Permanence and Responses
Sanusi enumerates the four objections of the majority and gives responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the four main objections raised by the majority against permanence as an additional attribute and understand the Ash'ari responses.

Core Idea
The majority raise four logical objections centered on existence-dependence and infinite regress.

Having presented the proof, the author now turns to the majority view that permanence is not additional. They give four objections that test the coherence of a separate permanence attribute. The author then responds to each, defending the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections sequentially
Read the passage from 'وذهب الأكثرون' to the end, noting each objection marked by 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
The objections are numbered; follow them in order.
2
First objection: permanence as continuation
Understand that the first objection equates permanence with continued existence, reducing it to existence in relation to time.
3
Second objection: dependence of permanence
The second objection argues that if permanence is an attribute, it would need a ground in the essence, leading to a chain of dependencies.
4
Third objection: circularity or multiplicity
Analyze the third objection: either permanence depends on essence (circular) or essence on permanence (making permanence necessary) or both independent (multiple necessary beings).
5
Fourth objection: infinite regress
The fourth objection says permanence would need its own permanence, leading to infinite regress and the problem of meaning subsisting in meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
The source argues that an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute (qiyam al-ma'na bi al-ma'na) and that this is unimaginable. How does this affect your understanding of God's simplicity? Are you willing to accept that God's attributes are distinct yet not separate, even if your mind cannot fully grasp it? What does this demand of your faith?

Refuting the Zahirite Denial of Attributes Beyond the Seven
The author refutes the Zahirite claim that there are no attributes beyond the seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Zahirite argument against attributes beyond seven and the refutation based on the principle that obligation is within capacity and that the Law is a valid path to knowledge of attributes.

Core Idea
Lack of evidence does not prove non-existence, and divine obligation is limited to human capacity.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a dispute among Ahl al-Haqq about divine attributes beyond the seven essential ones. Some Zahirites argued that no other attributes exist, giving two reasons. The author refutes them by denying the premises and showing the flaw in their reasoning. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter title and context
Begin by noting that this is the seventh chapter, dealing with attributes that are disputed among Ahl al-Haqq (those who affirm eternal attributes).
2
Identify the two Zahirite proofs
The Zahirites claim there are no attributes beyond seven based on (1) no evidence for them, so they must be negated, and (2) perfect knowledge obligation would require us to know them if they existed.
3
Examine the refutation of each proof
Sanusi refutes the first by denying the two premises (that lack of evidence necessitates negation). He refutes the second by denying that perfect knowledge is obligatory to that extent—obligation is within capacity—and also challenges the claim that perfect knowers did not know them.
4
Note the final appeal to the Law
Sanusi concludes by pointing out that the religious Law (shar') is a valid path to knowledge of attributes, contrary to the Zahirites' limited sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع : في صفات اختلف فيها ، يعني اختلف فيها أهل الحق القائلون بالصفات الأزلية . زعم بعض الظاهرية أنه لا صفة الله تعالى وراء السبعة المذكورة لوجهين : أحدهما : أنه لا دليل عليه وكل ما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه، ورد بمنع المقدمتين. وثانيهما: أنا مكلفون بكمال المعرفة، وذلك بمعرفة الذات وجميع الصفات فلم كانت له صفات أخرى لعرفناها معشر العارفين الكاملين واللازم منتف بالضرورة وبأنه لا طريق إلى معرفة الصفات سوى الاستدلال بالأفعال، والتنزيه عن النقائص، وهما لا يدلان على صفة أخرى، ورد بالمنع بل التكليف بقدر الوسع . ولو سلم فما أدريك أن الكاملين لم يعرفوا صفة أخرى ، ولا نسلم أنه لا طريق سوى ما ذكرتم . أليس الشرع طريقاً قويماً، وصراطاً مستقياً ؟
The seventh chapter: on attributes that are disputed, meaning they are disputed among Ahl al-Haqq who affirm the eternal attributes. Some of the Zahirites claimed that there is no attribute of God Almighty beyond the seven mentioned, for two reasons: One: that there is no evidence for it, and whatever has no evidence must be negated; it was refuted by denying the two premises. The second: that we are obligated to perfect knowledge, and that is by knowing the essence and all attributes; so if He had other attributes, we the perfect knowers would have known them; but the necessary consequence is non-existent necessarily, and because there is no path to knowledge of attributes except inference from actions and transcendence of imperfections, and these two do not indicate another attribute. It was refuted by denying the obligation, rather obligation is within capacity. And even if granted, what makes you think that the perfect knowers did not know another attribute? And we do not concede that there is no path except what you mentioned. Is not the Law a straight path and a clear way?
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage opens the seventh chapter on disputed attributes. The author clarifies that the dispute is among Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) who affirm eternal attributes. He then recounts the position of some Zahirites who deny any attributes beyond the seven known ones. They give two proofs: (1) lack of evidence requires negation; (2) perfect knowledge obligation would require us to know any additional attributes. The author refutes the first by denying that lack of evidence necessitates negation (the premises are denied). He refutes the second by denying the premise that perfect knowledge is obligatory to that extent—rather obligation is within capacity. He also challenges the claim that the perfect knowers did not know any other attribute, and denies that the only paths to knowledge are those mentioned, pointing to the religious law as a valid path.

Key Terms
الصفات الأزلية
الظاهرية
الاستدلال بالأفعال
التنزيه
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That lack of evidence is sufficient to deny a divine attribute.
Correction: Sanusi denies the premise that lack of evidence necessitates negation; absence of proof does not prove absence.

Checkpoint
On what basis did the Zahirites deny attributes beyond the seven?
A) They had positive evidence against them
B) Lack of evidence and obligation of perfect knowledge
C) The consensus of the companions
D) The impossibility of additional attributes

Key Takeaway
The Zahirite argument that lack of evidence necessitates denial is rejected; moreover, obligation to know attributes is within capacity, and the Law provides a path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard the argument that lack of evidence justifies denial. When have you dismissed a truth about Allah because you felt you lacked proof? What does this lesson demand you change in how you approach the unseen attributes of Allah?
?
The Zahirites assumed that perfect knowers would know every attribute if they existed. Are you secretly assuming that because you haven't experienced or understood a particular divine attribute, it must not be real? How will you actively seek knowledge of attributes like Allah's mercy or anger from the Law starting today?
?
This lesson reminds you that your obligation to know Allah is within your capacity. Have you settled for a minimal understanding of Allah's attributes, or are you striving to learn more through the Qur'an and Sunnah? What is one attribute you will study deeply this week, and how will you apply its meaning to your life?
